The Distance Argument: Finitude of Parts and Shape
Commentator argues that finitude of parts in dimensions implies finitude of shape, countering al-Nazzām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's argument that finitude of parts in three dimensions entails finitude of shape, refuting al-Nazzām's claim of actual infinity.

Core Idea
If parts are finite in each dimension, the overall shape must be finite.

The opponent argued that if the necessary consequence is false, the antecedent must also be false. The commentator counters by explaining that finitude of parts in all dimensions implies finitude of shape, challenging al-Nazzām's view that every distance is composed of infinite actual parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the distance argument passage
Read the Arabic and its translation. Note the structure: a conditional question ('if it is said...') followed by the commentator's response ('I say...').
Focus on the phrase 'تناهي الأجزاء في الامتدادات الثلاثة يستلزم تناهي الشكل'.
2
Understand the logical connection
The commentator argues that if the parts in the three dimensions (length, width, depth) are finite, then the shape must be finite. This is because the number of parts in the finite distance does not exceed the number of parts in the continuous dimensions.
This refutes the claim that distance is composed of infinite parts.
3
Consider al-Nazzām's position
Al-Nazzām asserts that every dimension contains an infinite number of parts actually existing. The commentator shows that this leads to the conclusion that every finite shape would have infinite parts, which is absurd.
Thus the original argument against the leap is upheld.

?

?

Reflect on one area where you act as though your time or energy is infinite—an area where you delay obedience or excuse sin. If every step in that area has a finite number, why are you not taking the first step toward change today?

Critique of the Leap: A Case of Stubbornness
Commentator criticizes the leap as stubbornness and offers a better alternative response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator considers the doctrine of the leap (al-ṭafra) to be a form of stubbornness (mukābara) and what the better response would be.

Core Idea
The leap is not a valid argument; it is stubbornness and a better response exists.

Al-Nazzām's leap was proposed to explain how a moving body traverses a distance without passing through intermediate points. The commentator argues that this is mere stubbornness, as it contradicts evident sense perception (e.g., a pen producing a continuous line). He then suggests a more coherent alternative response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the leap
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the commentator first says al-Nazzām has no need for this stubbornness, then gives an example to show it is stubbornness.
The example of the pen producing a black line without white gaps demonstrates that the leap contradicts observable reality.
2
Identify why it is stubbornness
The commentator indicates that the leap is a form of stubbornness because it contradicts sense perception. The pen example shows that we see a continuous line, not leaps.
This highlights the importance of empirical evidence in kalam.
3
Understand the better response
The commentator suggests that instead of claiming the leap, one could say 'the mover did not catch up with the stationary' as a necessary consequence that is already covered by the first objection. This is a more elegant and consistent refutation.
This shows the commentator's preference for logical economy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a belief you once held that contradicted clear evidence, yet you stubbornly defended it because it was convenient or comfortable? How does the commentator's condemnation of 'stubbornness' (al-mukābara) challenge you to re-examine your own intellectual honesty today?
?
The leap was a complicated theory invented to solve a problem, but it failed both empirically and logically. Are there areas in your own life where you overcomplicate to avoid a simple, uncomfortable truth? What would it take for you to let go of such a 'leap' and accept the straightforward answer?
?
When you are presented with a refutation that makes your argument redundant, do you humbly retract or do you persist for the sake of winning? Commit now to one area where you will practice intellectual humility before God, even if it means admitting you were wrong.

The Time Argument: Finite Time with Infinite Parts is Stubbornness
Commentator rejects the claim that finite time has infinite parts as stubbornness, citing intuitive reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator considers the claim that finite time contains infinite parts to be a form of stubbornness that contradicts intuitive reason.

Core Idea
Claiming that finite time contains infinite parts contradicts intuitive reason; it is also stubbornness.

The opponent attempted to defend the possibility of finite time having infinite parts. The commentator rejects this as another instance of stubbornness (mukābara), arguing that the intuition of the intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql) requires that time composed of finite successive instants is actually infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the time argument passage
Read the short Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the commentator calls it 'also stubbornness' and invokes 'بداهة العقل' (intuition of the intellect).
This passage is the conclusion of the series of refutations.
2
Understand the claim being refuted
The opponent claims that finite time contains infinite parts, and that the instants (ānāt) are finite. The commentator says this is stubbornness.
This position would require that infinite parts exist within a finite duration.
3
Grasp the commentator's counter-argument
The commentator asserts that intuition (badīha) dictates that time composed of finite instants that are successive is actually infinite. In other words, if instants are finite, then the time composed of them must be finite, but if time is finite, it cannot contain infinite parts.
This highlights the role of intuitive self-evident truths in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد أجيب : بأنه استبعاد إلخ والتحقيق أنه إن أريد الجزء المانع للاتصال فلا شك في انعدامه كما إذا كان التركيب من الأجزاء بالفعل وإن أريد بالجزء المانع التركيب وإن أريد الاتصال والتفريق فقوله وأجيب ، إلخ، أي لا نسلم أن المقاطع نفس الماء فهو يجتمع مع متمايزة في الخارج، بل تمايزها في الذهن بعد فرض القسمة . قوله : (وهو) كون تلك المسافة إلخ ) فإن قيل : بطلان اللازم المذكور إنما يستلزم بطلان تركب المسافة من أجزاء غير متناهية وكل مسافة متركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية . قلت : تناهي الأجزاء في الامتدادات الثلاثة يستلزم تناهي الشكل بناء على أن الأجزاء التي وسط المسافة المتناهية للأجزاء التي في الامتدادات الثلاثة المتصلة بعضها ببعض لايزيد عليها في العدد أنه لا يجوز أن يتصل بجزء واحد جزءان أو نقول : المراد كون المسافة من حيث هي مسافة أي من حيث وقع فيها الحركة متناهية والنظام يقول بعدم التناهي بالفعل في كل امتدادات غير متناهية إذ لو تناهت في امتداد بناء على أن جميع الانقسامات الممكنة عنده حاصلة بالفعل والانقسامات في كل امتداد غير متناهية إذ لو تناهت في امتداد لزم الجزء وما في حكمه . قوله : ( ولا حاجة له أي للنظام إلى هذه المكابرة وهي القول بالطفرة ومما يدل على كونه مكابرة إنما هذا القلم فيحصل خط السواد من غير أن يبقى في خلاله أجزاء بيض وليس كذلك وذلك لأن المقصود هاهنا هو إيراد لازم آخر باطل . فلو قال : لم يلحق المتحرك الساكن لكان هذا اللازم مندرجاً في اللازم الأول فلم يحصل المقصود هذا خلف . قوله : ( كذلك الزمان المتناهي مشتمل على أجزاء غير متناهية هذا مع القول بتناهي الآنات المتجددة مكابرة أيضاً فإن بداهة العقل يقتضي عدم تناهي الزمان المركب من الغير المتناهية المتتالية في التحقيق كما لا يخفى . الآنات
His saying: 'And it was answered: This is a remote possibility, etc.' The investigation: If what is meant by the part is that which prevents continuity, then there is no doubt of its non-existence, as when composition is from actual parts. But if what is meant by the part is that which prevents composition, and if what is meant is continuity and separation, then his saying 'and it was answered, etc.' means: We do not concede that the cross-sections are the same as the water; rather they are distinct in the mind after assuming division. His saying: 'That the distance, etc.' For if it is said: The falsity of the mentioned necessary consequence only entails the falsity of the distance being composed of infinite parts, and every distance is composed of infinite parts. I say: The finitude of parts in the three dimensions entails the finitude of the shape, based on the fact that the parts that are in the middle of the finite distance for the parts in the three continuous dimensions do not exceed them in number; it is not permissible for two parts to be attached to one part. Or we say: What is meant is that the distance qua distance, i.e., insofar as motion occurs in it, is finite, while al-Nazzām asserts actual infinity in every dimension, because if it were finite in one dimension, then based on the view that all possible divisions are actual, divisions in each dimension are infinite; thus if it were finite in a dimension, it would entail the part and what is like it. His saying: 'And he has no need, i.e., al-Nazzām, for this stubbornness, which is the doctrine of the leap'; and what indicates that it is stubbornness is that this pen—it produces a black line without leaving white parts in between, but that is not so. This is because the intention here is to present another false necessary consequence. For if he said: 'The mover did not catch up with the stationary,' this necessary consequence would be subsumed under the first necessary consequence, so the purpose would not be achieved; this is a fallacy. His saying: 'Similarly, finite time contains infinite parts'—this, along with the assertion of the finitude of successive instants, is also stubbornness, for the intuition of the intellect requires the infinity of time composed of finite instants in reality, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This is a detailed commentary on the previous arguments. It begins by investigating the response to the mosquito argument, clarifying that the 'part' in question is not the same as the water—the cross-sections are mental, not external. Then it addresses the second argument about the distance: if the necessary consequence is false, it only disproves the antecedent; but the commentator argues that finitude of parts in all dimensions implies finitude of shape, countering al-Nazzām's view. Then it criticizes al-‘Allāf's leap as stubbornness, offering a better explanation of why it is not a valid response. Finally, it critiques the response that finite time has infinite parts, calling it stubbornness because intuition dictates that time composed of finite instants is actually infinite. This shows the depth of kalam reasoning.

The Mutakallimun's Proof for Indivisible Parts from Motion
The mutakallimun prove motion and space are composed of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's proof that motion and space are composed of indivisible parts (atoms) based on the necessity of an indivisible present moment of motion.

Core Idea
Motion proves atoms: the present part of motion must be indivisible.

The mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) crafted a compelling argument for atomism based on the nature of motion. They reasoned that if motion exists, the part of it present at any given moment cannot be divided without contradiction. This passage presents that proof and extends it to show that space (the body traversed) must also consist of indivisible parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument. Note the structure: it begins with a conditional, then a necessary conclusion, then the proof of indivisibility, and finally the extension to distance.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow each conditional.
2
Identify the key premise about motion
The argument relies on the claim that motion is non-stationary (غير قار الذات). Understand that this means a moving object does not remain in one place; it continuously changes position, so any given moment of motion cannot be extended.
This is the crucial property that prevents division of the present part.
3
Trace the proof for indivisibility of the present part
Follow the logic: If the present part of motion could be divided, one subpart would be earlier and the other later, so they cannot both be present. This contradicts the definition of the present part. Hence the present part is indivisible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the correspondence between motion and distance
The argument then shows that because each part of motion corresponds to a part of distance, the indivisibility of motion parts implies the indivisibility of distance parts. The alternative formulation (aw naqulu) reinforces this by noting that if distance were divisible, motion would be divisible as well.
This step extends the conclusion from motion to space.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
The argument establishes that both motion and the body (distance) are composed of indivisible parts. Consider how this fits into the broader atomic theory of the mutakallimun.
This proof is foundational for their cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الرابع : حجة المتكلمين على التقسيم
كونه مستقبلاً، فإذا لم يوجد الحاضر لم يوجد شيء منهما قطعاً، فلا وجود للحركة وهو باطل بالضرورة ، فوجب أن تكون الحاضرة منها موجودة ( وأنها لا تنقسم) بوجه ولو فرضاً ، ( وإلا لكان بعض أجزائها المفروضة قبل ، وبعضها بعد لأنها ) أي الحركة ( غير قار الذات ضرورة ) فإذا فرض فيها جزآن امتنع أن يكونا مجتمعين ( فلا يكون كلها (حاضراً بل بعضها (هذا خلف) لأن المقدر خلافه (وكذا جميع أجزائها ) غير قابلة للانقسام إذ ما من جزء من أجزائها ( إلا وكان حاضراً حيناً ما فثبت أن الحركة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فكذا المسافة التي هي الجسم مركبة منها أيضاً ) لانطباقها ( أي انطباق الحركة ( عليها ) بحيث إذا فرض في إحداهما جزء يفرض بإزائه من الأخرى جزء، فإذا كانت أجزاء الحركة غير قابلة للانقسام كانت أجزاء المسافة كذلك (أو نقول : ( يجب أن تكون أجزاء المسافة غير منقسمة ) لأنه لو انقسمت المسافة ) التي يقع عليها جزء من أجزاء الحركة ( لانقسمت الحركة عليها ) أعني ذلك الجزء من الحركة ) فإن الحركة إلى نصفها أي نصف المسافة (نصف الحركة إليها).
The First Observatory - The Fourth Objective: The Argument of the Theologians on Divisibility. Since it is future, if the present does not exist, then none of them exist definitely, so there is no existence of motion, and that is necessarily false. So the present [part] of it must exist, and it does not divide in any way, even hypothetically; for if it did, some of its supposed parts would be before and some after, because motion is non-stationary in essence necessarily. So if two parts are supposed in it, it is impossible for them to be together; so not all of it is present, but only some of it. That is a contradiction because the estimation is contrary to that. Likewise, all its parts are indivisible, since there is no part of it except that it was present at some time. So it is established that motion is composed of indivisible parts, and likewise the distance which is the body is composed of them also, because of the correspondence of motion to it, such that if a part is supposed in one of them, a part is supposed corresponding to it from the other. So if the parts of motion are indivisible, then the parts of distance are likewise. Or we say: It is necessary that the parts of distance be indivisible, because if the distance upon which a part of the parts of motion occurs were to divide, then the motion upon it—meaning that part of motion—would divide; for motion to half of it, i.e., half of the distance, is half of the motion to it.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a proof by the mutakallimun (theologians) for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms) in motion and consequently in space (the body). The argument begins by considering motion in terms of past, present, and future. It assumes that motion exists and is extended in time. The argument runs: If there were no present part of motion, then no part of motion would exist at all, which is false because motion does exist. Therefore, the present part of motion must exist. This present part must be indivisible, because if it could be divided, one part would be earlier and the other later, given that motion is essentially non-stationary (it does not endure). Since they cannot coexist, not all of it would be present, contradicting the assumption that it is the present part. Hence every part of motion that exists at a given moment is indivisible. Since motion corresponds to the distance (space) it traverses—each part of motion corresponding to a part of distance—the parts of distance must also be indivisible. A second formulation (aw naqūlu) says: If a part of distance were divisible, then the corresponding part of motion would also divide (since motion to half the distance is half the motion), which contradicts the indivisibility of the motion parts. Thus both motion and space are composed of indivisible parts.

Key Terms
الحركة
المسافة
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
غير قار الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument assumes motion is a single continuous entity extended in time, and that dividing it would imply discontinuity, but the argument actually uses the non-stationary nature to show that any division leads to temporal separation, so the present part cannot be split.
Correction: The argument does not rely on continuity but on the fact that motion does not endure (non-stationary), so any division would create a before and after, making it impossible for both subparts to be present simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What does the mutakallimun's argument from motion primarily establish?
A) That motion is continuous and infinitely divisible
B) That motion and space are composed of indivisible parts
C) That motion exists only in the mind
D) That space is infinite

Key Takeaway
Motion and space are composed of indivisible parts, as proven by the logical necessity of an indivisible present moment of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe you exist and act in the present. The mutakallimun argued that if there were no indivisible present moment of motion, nothing would exist. Are you living as if each present moment is an indivisible unit of accountability, or do you let distractions split your attention and excuse your negligence? How will you treat the very next present moment as whole and undivided in your worship?
?
The proof shows that a part of distance cannot be divided without dividing the corresponding part of motion. In your spiritual journey, have you compartmentalized your actions—thinking some parts are insignificant—while the mutakallimun insist every part corresponds to a real indivisible unit? What small, seemingly divisible action will you commit to performing with the understanding that it is indivisible and wholly accountable?
?
If the present part of motion did not exist, the entire motion would be lost. Consider your life as a motion toward Allah. Are you investing in the present moment as the only part that truly exists? Pause right now: what is the one present action you can take to move closer to your Creator, and will you treat that action as indivisible and non-negotiable?

Al-Razi's Objection and the Response on Motion
Al-Razi's objection and mutakallimun's response on motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection against the proof for indivisible parts of motion and how the mutakallimun respond by appealing to the continuity of motion and the role of the present moment.

Core Idea
Motion's continuity and the present moment as a boundary refute al-Razi's criticism.

Imam al-Razi presents what he considers the strongest argument against the mutakallimun's claim that motion is composed of indivisible parts. He distinguishes two senses of motion and argues that neither yields composition. The sharh (commentary) responds by clarifying that motion is continuous and the present moment is a shared boundary, so the objection does not stand. This lesson presents both the objection and the rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's Objection
Carefully read the first part of the Arabic passage where al-Razi distinguishes two types of motion and argues that neither supports the existence of indivisible parts.
Notice he defines 'traversal' (qaṭʿ) and 'being in-between' (tawassuṭ).
2
Identify the Two Meanings of Motion
Al-Razi says 'traversal' has no real existence; 'being in-between' exists in the present moment but does not correspond to distance. Understand why he thinks this disproves composition.
3
Read the Sharh's Response
Read the response: motion is continuous; when divided by estimation, parts occur in their own times, and the present moment is a boundary preventing motion from occurring in it.
The response argues that non-existence in the present does not imply non-existence absolutely.
4
Understand the Role of the Present Moment
The present moment (al-ān al-ḥāḍir) is the common boundary between past and future. Motion does not occur in this boundary, but it exists in its own time.
This is key to refuting the objection.
5
Connect to the Bigger Picture
See how this exchange fits into the larger debate about indivisible parts. The sharh upholds the original proof while showing al-Razi's argument is not decisive.
The response also mentions a second proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي : هذا أقوى ما احتج به مثبتو الجزء ويرد عليه أن الحركة بمعنى القطع لا وجود لها أصلاً كما مر والحركة بمعنى التوسط موجودة في الآن الحاضر لكنها ليست منطبقة على المسافة إذ لا جزء لها في امتداد المسافة بل هي موجودة في كل حد من الحدود المفروضة فيها فليس لنا حركة مركبة من أجزاء

المصنف من أن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل ما سيحصر كما أن تقرير المصنف لا يحتاج إلى الله أخذ أن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان . والشارح رحمه جمع بين المقدمتين لزيادة الإيضاح . والجواب عن هذه الحجة ظاهر لأن الحركة متصلة في نفسها إذا قسمها الوهم باعتبار الزمان حصل فيه جزءان كل منهما واقع في زمانه والآن الحاضر الحد المشترك بين ذينك الزمانين يمنع وقوع الحركة فيه، فالقول بكون الحركة منقسمة إلى الحاضرة والمستقبلة وأن عدم وجودها في الحاضر يستلزم عدمها مطلقاً، وأن الماضي كان حاضراً والمستقبل لم يوجد فإنه لا يلزم من عدمها في الحال عدمها مطلقاً فإنهما موجودان في زمانهما . قوله : ( أو نقول إلخ) فالأول كان إثباتاً لتركب المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ بطريق الاستقامة وهذا إثبات بطريق الخلف
قوله : ( لا وجود له أصلاً كما مر أي في المقصد الثاني من مباحث الأين على رأي الحكماء وفي مباحث الزمان أيضاً على أن الشارح صرح هناك بأن الحكماء لا يثبتون الحاضر من الزمان بل الحاضر عندهم هو الزمان الموهوم الذي هو قدر مشترك بينهما بمنزلة النقطة المفروضة على المطلوب ليس جزءاً من الزمان أصلاً إلخ، فليرجع إليها ليطلع على فوائد جمة وعوائد كثيرة .
لا تتجزأ نعم يرتسم من هذه الحركة الموجودة في الخارج أمر ممتد في الخيال منطبق على المسافة منقسم مثلها إلى أجزاء لا تقف على حد لا يقبل الانقسام.
Imam al-Razi said: This is the strongest argument adduced by the affirmers of the part, and it is refuted by the fact that motion in the sense of traversal has no existence at all, as has passed, and motion in the sense of being in-between exists in the present moment, but it does not correspond to distance, since it has no part in the extension of distance; rather, it exists in every one of the supposed limits in it, so we do not have a motion composed of parts. ... The commentator, may God have mercy on him, combined the two premises for greater clarification. And the answer to this argument is apparent: because motion is continuous in itself; when the estimation divides it with respect to time, two parts occur, each occurring in its own time, and the present moment is the common boundary between those two times, preventing the occurrence of motion in it. So the statement that motion is divided into present and future, and that its non-existence in the present entails its non-existence absolutely, and that the past was present and the future has not existed—it does not follow from its non-existence in the current state that it does not exist absolutely, for they exist in their respective times. ... As for his saying 'or we say etc.', the first was a proof of the composition of distance from indivisible parts by direct method, and this is a proof by way of contradiction. ... It is not divisible; rather, from this motion existing externally there is depicted in the imagination an extended thing corresponding to distance, divisible like it into parts that do not stop at a limit that does not accept division.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Imam al-Razi to the mutakallimun's argument for indivisible parts, followed by the sharh's (commentator's) response. Al-Razi distinguishes two meanings of motion: traversal (qaṭʿ) which is not real, and being in-between (tawassuṭ) which exists in the present instant but does not correspond to distance, so motion is not composite. The sharh explains that the objection is based on misunderstanding: motion is continuous; when imagination divides it by time, you get parts each in its own time, and the present instant is a boundary preventing motion from occurring in it. Thus non-existence in the present does not imply non-existence absolutely. The sharh adds that the first proof (direct) and the second (by contradiction) both stand. On page 17, the sharh further clarifies that from external motion an extended thing is depicted in the imagination, which is divisible but not into an infinite number of parts—this resolves the objection.

Key Terms
الحركة بمعنى القطع
الحركة بمعنى التوسط
الآن الحاضر
انطباق الحركة
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's objection proves that motion cannot be composed of parts, thereby refuting the mutakallimun's argument for indivisible parts.
Correction: The response shows that the objection is based on a misunderstanding of the present moment's role; motion's continuity and time-boundary nature mean the objection does not succeed.

Checkpoint
According to the sharh's response, why does motion not exist in the present moment?
A) Because motion is unreal
B) Because the present moment is a boundary, not a duration
C) Because motion is composed of indivisible parts that cannot fit in an instant
D) Because the present moment is only a part of time

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objection fails because it treats the present moment as a container for motion, whereas the mutakallimun see it as a boundary; motion exists in its own time, not in the boundary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your daily actions—walking, speaking, even breathing—do you experience them as continuous flows or as discrete moments? This lesson teaches that the present moment is a boundary, not a container for motion. How does this challenge the way you perceive your own existence in time? Will you now treat each 'now' as a limit between what you have done and what you will do, prompting you to act with greater intentionality?
?
If the present moment cannot hold any action or motion, then what is the 'now' you live in? Is it merely a thought, a point of transition? How does this shift your understanding of the preciousness of time? Could it be that you have been mistakenly clinging to the present as if it were a possession, when in fact it is only a bridge? What changes would you make to ensure you are not wasting the only thing you truly have—the past you shape and the future you build?

Euclid's Smallest Angle and Its Refutation
Euclid's proof of an indivisible angle and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Euclid's argument for an indivisible angle and why kalam scholars refuted it by showing that the angle is only relatively smallest, not absolutely smallest.

Core Idea
Euclid's 'smallest angle' is not absolutely smallest; it is divisible.

Euclid, the ancient Greek mathematician, presented a proof that a certain angle formed by a tangent line and a circle is the smallest angle and therefore indivisible. This was used as an argument for the existence of indivisible parts (atoms). However, the kalam scholars responded by clarifying the scope of Euclid's conclusion. In this lesson, you will examine Euclid's proof and the rejoinder that shows why it does not prove indivisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how the proof is presented and then answered.
The Arabic contains nested commentary; focus on the main flow.
2
Identify Euclid's claim
Euclid claims there is an angle formed by a straight line tangent to a circle that is the smallest of all angles, hence indivisible. Understand why indivisibility follows.
Indivisibility is argued because if divisible, the angle would not be smallest.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer (al-jawāb) clarifies that Euclid's proof only shows the angle is smaller than acute angles with straight sides, not absolutely smaller than all acute angles. This leaves room for smaller angles from different circles.
4
Examine the explanation with circles
The commentary explains that an angle from a larger circle is smaller than that from a smaller circle. Thus, the angle varies and is not absolutely smallest.
Sound imagination (takhayyul) is invoked.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for kalam: if the angle is divisible, it does not prove an indivisible part, thus refuting a key proof for atomism.
This connects to the broader discussion of indivisible parts.

الوجه الثالث : برهن إقليدس ) في الشكل الخامس عشر من المقالة الثالثة من الأصول ( على وجود زاوية هي أصغر الزوايا وهي ما تحصل من مماسة خط مستقيم) لمحيط دائرة فهي ( لا تنقسم إذ لو انقسمت لم تكن أصغر الزوايا ) ولا تتصور ) الزاوية التي لا تنقسم (إلا) بإثبات الجزء لأن تلك الزاوية إن كانت جوهراً كانت جزءاً، وإن كانت عرضاً فلا بد لها من محل هو جوهر غير منقسم، والجواب : أن المبرهن في كتابه هو أن الزاوية الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة والخط المماس لها أصغر من كل زاوية حادة مستقيمة الخطين لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد.
قوله : (لا أنها أصغر إلخ) فهي قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية ويحصل بالقسمة زاوية بين محيط الدائرة والخط المستقيم أصغر منها .
قوله : (هو أنه الزاوية الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة الحدبة بالنقاط الثلاث، وذكر في الصحاح أن الحدب ما ارتفع من الأرض والحدبة التي في الظهر يعني أنا نفرض دائرة يماس حدبتها خطاً مستقيماً بنقطة في وسط هذا الخط فيحدث هناك زاويتان حادتان. ولا شك أن كل واحدة منهما تكون أصغر من كل حادة مستقيمة الضلعين إذا فرض تساويهما في الضلعين والوتر جميعاً، وقوله : لا أنها أصغر من جميع الحواد إذ لا شك أن الحادة الحادثة من حدبة الدائرة الحادة الحادثة الخط المستقيم أصغر من مع ذلك الخط من حدبة الدائرة الصغرى مع المستقيم أيضاً فإن أحد ضلعي الحادة الأولى يكون بين ضلعي الحادة الثانية، فيكون وتر الثانية أطول من وتر الأولى كما يشهد به التخيل الصحيح. الكبرى قوله : (لا أنها أصغر من جميع (الحواد) كما يظهر من أطراف المتممات وأنها أيضاً متفاوتة .
The third aspect: Euclid proved in the fifteenth figure of the third article of the Elements the existence of an angle that is the smallest of angles, which is obtained from the tangency of a straight line to the circumference of a circle. So it is indivisible, for if it were divisible, it would not be the smallest of angles. And the angle that is indivisible is not conceivable except by affirming the part, because if that angle is a substance, it is a part, and if it is an accident, it must have a substratum that is an indivisible substance. The answer: That which is proved in his book is that the acute angle arising from the convexity of the circle and the tangent line is smaller than every acute angle with straight sides, not that it is smaller than all acute angles. His statement: 'Not that it is smaller etc.' So it is divisible infinitely, and by division an angle between the circumference and the straight line smaller than it is obtained. His statement: 'It is that the acute angle arising from the convexity of the circle...' means we suppose a circle whose convexity is touched by a straight line at a point in the middle of this line, so two acute angles occur there. There is no doubt that each of them is smaller than every acute angle with equal straight sides if we suppose them equal in both sides and the hypotenuse. And his statement: 'not that it is smaller than all acute angles' because there is no doubt that the acute angle arising from the convexity of the larger circle with the straight line is smaller than that arising from the convexity of the smaller circle with the same line, since one side of the first angle lies between the two sides of the second, so the hypotenuse of the second is longer than that of the first, as sound imagination testifies. The major [premise?] his statement: 'not that it is smaller than all acute angles' as appears from the ends of the complements, and that they also vary.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for indivisible parts, attributed to Euclid, followed by a refutation. The proof claims there exists an angle formed by a tangent line and a circle that is the smallest angle, hence indivisible, and that such an indivisible angle implies an indivisible substance (either as a substance itself or as an accident in an indivisible substratum). The answer (al-jawāb) clarifies that Euclid's proof only shows this angle is smaller than any straight-sided acute angle, not absolutely smaller than all acute angles (including those formed by circles of different sizes). The commentator (sharh) explains that this angle is actually divisible infinitely, as a smaller angle can be obtained by division. A further commentary elaborates: the angle from a larger circle is smaller than that from a smaller circle, so the angle is not absolutely smallest. Thus the proof fails to establish an indivisible part.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Euclid's angle is absolutely smallest and therefore indivisible.
Correction: The angle is only smallest among straight-sided acute angles. It is divisible, as a smaller angle can be obtained from a larger circle or by division.

C) It uses a circle which does not exist
D) It confuses angles with lines

Key Takeaway
Euclid's proof does not establish an indivisible angle because the angle is only smallest among straight-sided acute angles and can still be divided.

?

The Fourth Proof: Sphere and Plane Contact
A proof for indivisible parts from sphere-plane contact, with objection and rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contact between a sphere and a plane is used to prove the existence of indivisible parts, and how the commentator defends this proof against skeptical objections.

Core Idea
Sphere-plane contact forces an indivisible point, proving indivisible parts exist.

This passage presents one of the kalam arguments for atomism, using geometry. The proof assumes a real sphere touching a real plane; the point of contact must be an indivisible part. The commentator addresses an objection from Avicenna about whether such a configuration is possible, and defends the possibility by pointing to natural spheres (celestial bodies) and the nature of surfaces.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof's assumption and conclusion
Read the Arabic and English carefully. The proof begins by supposing a real sphere touching a real plane. Under the opponent's denial of indivisible parts, contact must occur at an indivisible point, thus proving the part.
The phrase 'فما' is incomplete but indicates the logical consequence.
2
Examine the objection from al-Shifa
The passage quotes al-Shifa, questioning whether such a sphere and plane can actually exist in reality or only in estimation. This objection challenges the validity of the proof.
Avicenna also questions whether rolling would be possible if it exists.
3
Analyze the commentator's rebuttal
The commentator argues that denying the possibility is obstinacy. He points out that natural simple bodies (like celestial spheres) are real spheres, and plane surfaces exist; if a surface is not plane, it must eventually become plane to avoid infinite angles.
This rebuttal grounds the proof in observable realities.

الوجه الرابع : نفرض كرة ) حقيقية ) تماس سطحاً مستوياً حقيقياً ( لإمكان الكرة والسطح ) المذكورين ( وتماسها ضرورة على ( تقدير انتفاء الجزء كما هو مذهب الخصم ( فما

قوله : ( لا مكان إلخ) في الشفاء : لا يدري هل يمكن أنه يوجد كرة على السطح بهذه الصفة في الوجود أو هو في التوهم فقط على نحو ما عليه التعليمات فلا يدري أنه إن كان في الوجود هل يصح مدحرجة أو لا عليه انتهى ولا خفاء في أن منع إمكان وجود الكرة والسطح مكابرة لأن الشكل الطبيعي للبسيط الكرة بل واقعة لأن الأفلاك عندهم كرات حقيقية كذا وجود السطح المستوي لأنه لا شك في وجود السطح فإن كان مستوياً فهو المطلوب وإن كان ذوات زوايا فلا بد من الانتهاء إليه لامتناع اشتماله على السطوح وزوايا غير متناهية وقد مر ذلك في بحث الخلاء .
The fourth aspect: We suppose a real sphere touching a real plane surface, because of the possibility of the mentioned sphere and surface, and their contact necessarily upon the assumption of the absence of the part, as is the doctrine of the opponent. Then what? His statement: 'Regarding possibility etc.' in al-Shifa: It is not known whether it is possible that a sphere exists on the surface with this description in existence, or it is only in estimation, similar to what is in the Instructions; so it is not known that if it exists, whether rolling is correct or not, end of quote. And it is no secret that denying the possibility of the existence of the sphere and surface is obstinacy, because the natural form of a simple body is the sphere, indeed they occur, for the celestial spheres according to them are real spheres, and likewise the existence of a plane surface, because there is no doubt about the existence of a surface; if it is plane, it is the desired [thing], and if it has angles, then one must end at it, because it is impossible for it to contain infinite surfaces and angles, and that has passed in the discussion of void.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for indivisible parts, based on the contact between a sphere and a plane. The argument assumes it is possible to have a real sphere and a real plane surface; if the sphere touches the plane at a single point (assuming no indivisible part), then the contact would be at an indivisible point, implying existence of an indivisible part. The commentator (sharh) cites al-Shifa (Avicenna) questioning whether such a sphere and surface can actually exist in reality or only in estimation. However, the sharh then rejects this skepticism as obstinacy (mukābara), arguing that spheres naturally exist (like celestial spheres) and plane surfaces exist; if a surface is not plane, it must eventually end at a plane to avoid infinite surfaces and angles. This supports the possibility of the contact and thus the argument for indivisible parts.

The Objection and Answer on Tangency in Rolling Motion
Refutes the objection that sphere rolling requires point-to-point tangency, proving no infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun refute the objection that rolling motion implies an infinite regress of points by appealing to the compound nature of rolling motion.

Core Idea
Rolling motion's dual components allow tangency to persist, avoiding infinite regress of points.

The mutakallimun's argument for the composition of the line from points faces an objection about sphere rolling. Critics argue that if a sphere rolls, it must be tangent to each point successively, leading to an infinite number of contact points. This passage presents the objection and the sophisticated reply that the sphere's combined rectilinear and circular motion allows the same point to remain in contact for a duration, thus requiring no infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection (beginning with 'لا يقال') to understand the critic's argument: that rolling requires tangency at successive points, leading to an infinite regress.
Notice the two alternatives presented: tangency at first point (sphere stationary) or at an intermediate point (infinite points).
2
Identify the answer's strategy
The answer first denies the necessity of stationariness by introducing the concept of compound motion. Look for the phrase 'لأنا نقول' and follow the explanation.
The key insight is that rolling motion is both rectilinear and circular.
3
Understand how compound motion solves the problem
The circular motion allows the sphere to maintain tangency at the same point on the surface for a duration, while the rectilinear motion gradually moves the sphere forward. This means point-to-point tangency is not instantaneous or infinite.
Reflect on the 'true imagination' of rolling motion mentioned at the end.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الرابع: حجة المتكلمين على التقسيم

لا يقال : فعلى ما ذكرت لا تحصل المماسة على النقطة الأخرى إلا بعد الحركة ففي حال الحركة لا بد من المماسة فإن كانت المماسة على النقطة الأولى كانت الكرة ساكنة حال كونها ،متحركة وإن كانت على نقطة متوسطة بينهما لزم خلاف المقدر على أنا ننقل الكلام إلى تلك المتوسطة فوجب إذن أن لا يكون بين نقطتي التماس واسطة فيلزم تتالي النقط لأنا نقول : المماسة على النقطة الأولى، وإن كانت

قوله : ( كانت الكرة ساكنة لعدم التغير من الحالة الأولى حال كونها متحركة لأن المفروض أن حال الحركة خلاف المقدر لأن المقدر أن المماسة على النقطة الثانية .

قوله : ( لأنا نقول المماسة إلخ ) هذا اختيار للشق الأول ومنع الملازمة قوله : كانت الكرة

ساكنة حال كونها متحركة وقوله : لكنها باقية في زمان حركة الدحرجة ولعل السر في ذلك هو حركة الكرة المذكورة على السطح المذكور كانت مركبة من الحركة المستقيمة والحركة المستديرة معاً فباعتبار الحركة المستديرة يتصور أن تبقى المسامتة على نقطة واحدة من السطح زماناً وباعتبار الحركة المستقيمة يتصور أن تزول تلك المسامتة بحيث لا تبقى هناك، هذا ولكن بقي أن يقال : إنك قد أقررت أنه لا بد أن يكون بين النقطتين خط فحركة الكرة على هذا الخط إما أن يكون لا بالمماسة وهو باطل لأنه خلاف المفروض ، وإما أن يكون بالمماسة وهذه المماسة يتصور أن تكون على النقطة الأولى أو على النقطة الثانية إذ المفروض هو أن يكون الحركة على الخط فيما بين النقطتين فتعين أن المماسة كانت على نقطة متوسطة بينهما فحينئذ يلزم ما ذكر من أنه خلاف المفروض وأنه ينقل الكلام إلى تلك المتوسطة، فتأمل .

The First Observatory - The Fourth Goal: The Proof of the Mutakallimun on Division. It is not said: According to what you mentioned, tangency at the other point does not occur except after motion; so during motion there must be tangency. If tangency was at the first point, the sphere would be stationary while it is moving; and if it was at an intermediate point between them, it would contradict the assumption; moreover, we transfer the discussion to that intermediate point, so it would necessarily follow that there is no interval between the two points of tangency, hence the succession of points. So we say: Tangency at the first point, although it is... His saying: (According to what you mentioned does not occur etc.) The correct thing is that tangency does occur, because it is what was mentioned and not dependent on it previously. His saying: (The sphere would be stationary due to no change from the first state while it is moving) because the assumption is that the state of motion contradicts the assumption because the assumption is that tangency is at the second point. His saying: (We transfer the discussion etc.) because it also after motion, so during motion the tangency is at another point that mediates between the first and the first intermediate, and so on until it leads to the existence of infinite tangencies and points while they are bounded between two boundaries. Rather we say: All these infinite tangencies occur after motion, so during motion there must be another tangency; thus the totality is not all at once. His saying: (Tangency at the first point etc.) is a denial of the implication derived from his saying: If the tangency was at the first point, the sphere would be stationary while it is moving; meaning we do not accept the necessity of its being stationary while it is moving, because the tangency of the sphere at the specific point of the surface, being indivisible, persists for the duration of the rolling motion until tangency occurs at the other specific point of the surface, because the sphere moves on itself so its points change while the tangency at the first point of the surface remains. When it reaches the second point of the surface, another tangency occurs that persists with the sphere's motion on itself until the third point of the surface is reached, and so on. There is a discussion in it: firstly, because rolling is a compound motion... His saying: (For we say tangency etc.) is a choice of the first side and a denial of the implication. His saying: (the sphere was stationary while it is moving) and his saying: (but it persists for the duration of the rolling motion) and perhaps the secret in that is that the mentioned motion of the sphere on the mentioned surface was composed of rectilinear motion and circular motion together. With respect to circular motion, it is conceivable that the alignment remains on a single point of the surface for a time, and with respect to rectilinear motion, it is conceivable that that alignment disappears so that it does not remain there. This but there remains to be said: You have acknowledged that there must be a line between the two points; so the motion of the sphere on this line is either not by tangency, which is false because it contradicts the assumption, or by tangency, and this tangency is conceivable to be at the first point or at the second point, since the assumption is that motion is on the line between the two points. Then it is determined that the tangency was at an intermediate point between them, and then what was mentioned follows: that it contradicts the assumption and that the discussion is transferred to that intermediate point. So reflect. It occurs in an instant but it persists for the duration of the rolling motion leading to tangency at the other point. So in the instant of the occurrence of this second tangency, the first tangency disappears. And so every tangency at a point occurs in an instant or persists for a time, and that does not contradict the continuity of the sphere's motion, as appears from the true imagination of the rolling motion. Thus the succession of points and instants does not necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection against the mutakallimun's proof for the composition of the line from points. The objector argues: based on the assumption that a sphere rolls on a surface, tangency at a new point occurs only after motion; thus during motion there must be a tangency at the first point? But that would imply the sphere is stationary while moving. Alternatively, if tangency is at an intermediate point, that contradicts the assumption that the points are adjacent. The answer first denies the implication: tangency at the first point can persist through time because the sphere's motion is compound (rectilinear and circular). During rolling, the sphere rotates on its axis, so the points on the sphere change while the point of contact on the surface remains the same. The commentator (Qadhib al-Din al-Razi? or al-Jurjani?) explains that the rolling motion consists of two components: circular motion (which keeps the same point of contact on the surface for a duration) and rectilinear motion (which gradually changes that point). Thus no infinite regress of points occurs. The objection is further answered by noting that tangency occurs in an instant or persists, and the continuity of motion does not require successive points. Therefore the argument for atomism is not undermined.

Correction: The passage clarifies that due to the circular component of rolling, the same point of contact can persist for a duration, avoiding infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The rolling motion's circular component allows a single point of contact to persist, thus no infinite succession of points is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about how something must work—like how two objects interact—only to realize that a deeper understanding of its components (like compound motion) changes everything? What hidden component might you be missing in your own understanding of your faith that could resolve a seeming contradiction you face? Take time this week to identify one such contradiction and actively search for the missing piece.
?
When you encounter a logical objection to your beliefs that seems airtight, do you dismiss it immediately or do you seek the 'compound motion'—the hidden perspective that might resolve it? Think of a specific doubt you've had. Did you honestly look for that hidden component, or did you avoid it? Commit to examining one such objection this month with the same rigor as the mutakallimun examining rolling motion.
?
Your soul is in constant motion—through trials, sins, and repentance. Do you believe that God's mercy can be constant, like the point of tangency, even as you change? Or do you feel you must perfectly align with Him at every instant for His mercy to touch you? Are you willing to let that mercy persist while you are in motion, trusting that the rotation of your life does not break the contact? Make a concrete decision this week to rest in the constancy of His mercy despite your imperfections.

Fifth Proof: The Line Passing Over Another Implies Points
Presents the fifth proof that a line is composed of points by considering a line moving over another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for the composition of lines from points and the objection regarding accidents and motion.

Core Idea
A moving line touching another line forces both to be made of points.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the fifth argument that a line must be composed of discrete points. The proof relies on the nature of contact between a moving line and a stationary line. An objection arises from the fact that a line is an accident, not a substance, and therefore cannot truly move. The response clarifies the continuous nature of motion and the role of points as theoretical constructs. This lesson walks you through the proof, the objection, and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof setup
Understand the hypothesis: a line stands perpendicular on another line and passes over it. The first line touches the second during its motion.
The tangency is claimed to occur only at a point.
2
Trace the conclusion and extension
From the point of tangency, deduce that the stationary line is composed of points. Then extend analogically: surfaces are composed of lines, bodies of surfaces.
This establishes the atomist composition hierarchy.
3
Examine the objection and response
Note the objection: a moving thing must be a substance (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), but a line is an accident, so how can it move? Then follow the response that motion is continuous and points are not actual until an instant is posited.
The response resolves the tension without denying the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الخامس : نفرض خطاً قائماً على خط ويمر) الخط الأول ( عليه ) أي على الخط الثاني ( فإنه يماس ( الخط المار ( في مروره جميع أجزاء ذلك ) الخط الممرور عليه أو المماسة ) بينهما ( إنما تكون بنقطة ) لأن المماس من الخط القائم المار هو طرفه النقطة الذي هو وممسوس النقطة لا يكون إلا نقطة ) فالخط الممرور عليه مركب من نقط) متتالية (و) كذلك (السطح) مركب من خطوط متلاقية (والجسم) مركب ) من سطوح ) مجتمعة ، ( وهو المطلوب ( ويتجه عليه أن المتحرك هو المتحيز بالذات فلا بد أن يكون منقسماً في جميع الجهات كما سيأتي فالسطح والخط والنقطة لا تكون إلا أعراضاً فكيف يتصور حركة خط عرضي على آخر مثله. قوله : ( ويتجه عليه إلخ ) وهكذا يتجه عليه أن الحركة متصلة منطبقة على المسافة المتصلة ليس فيها النقطة بالفعل إلا بعد فرض الآن في الزمان فتبين أن كل نقطتين مفروضتين خط كما أن بين كل آنين زمان وبين كل جزأين حصول في حد قوله : ( فلا بد وأن يكون منقسماً في جميع الجهات كما سيأتي) أي في أول مقصد يليه.
The fifth aspect: We hypothesize a line standing on a line and passing over it; the first line (over it) meaning over the second line; for it touches (the passing line) (in its passage all parts of that) (the line passed over or the tangency) (between them) (is only at a point) because the tangent from the standing passing line is its endpoint, the point, which and the point of tangency is only a point. So the line passed over is composed of successive points, and similarly the surface is composed of intersecting lines, and the body is composed of aggregated surfaces, and that is what is required. And it is objected against it that the moving thing is essentially spatial (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat), so it must be divisible in all directions as will come; thus the surface, line, and point are only accidents; so how can it be conceived that an accidental line moves over another like it? His saying: (And it is objected against it etc.) And thus it is objected against it that motion is continuous and coincident with the continuous distance, in which there is no point in actuality except after positing the instant in time; so it becomes clear that every two hypothesized points have a line, just as between every two instants there is time, and between every two parts there is occurrence at the boundary. His saying: (so it must be divisible in all directions as will come) i.e., at the beginning of the goal that follows.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the composition of the line from points. The argument: suppose a line (line A) stands perpendicular on another line (line B) and passes over it. During its motion, line A touches line B. The point of contact can only be a point (since the tangent from the moving line is its endpoint, which is a point). Therefore, line B (passed over) must be composed of discrete points, because it is touched at each point successively. Similarly, a surface is composed of lines (by extension) and a body of surfaces. This proves the atomist position. The passage then includes an objection: the moving object must be a substance (mutahayyiz bi al-dhat) divisible in all directions; but the line, surface, and point are accidents, not substances. How can an accident (a line) move over another accident? The commentator (al-Jurjani) responds by noting that motion is continuous and does not have actual points except when an instant is considered; thus, between any two points there is a line, and the argument stands. This shows the interplay between kalam and falsafa.

Key Terms
الخط القائم
المماسة
النقطة
الخط الممرور عليه
المنطبق
المتحيز بالذات
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moving line must be a substance (mutahayyiz) for the proof to work.
Correction: The proof does not require actual substantial motion; it is a thought experiment about contact, and the response clarifies that motion is continuous and points are mind-dependent.

Checkpoint
In the fifth proof, what does the contact between the moving line and the stationary line imply?
A) The stationary line is infinitely divisible.
B) The stationary line is composed of discrete points.
C) The moving line is a substance.
D) Contact is impossible between two lines.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof uses the idea of contact between moving lines to argue for atomic composition, despite the objection about accidental motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even a line, which we perceive as continuous, must be made of discrete points. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you assume is seamless but, upon reflection, you see is made up of small, discrete actions? What is one step you will take today to acknowledge that granularity and act intentionally on that smallest unit?
?
The objection says motion requires a substance, not an accident like a line. How often do you attribute reality to things that are merely accidental? In your spiritual life, what is the substance (like intention or tawheed) that your actions must be grounded in? Where have you been treating accidents as if they were substances?
?
If the universe's structure is so precise that even a line is composed of points, does that deepen your awe of the Creator who designed it in such detail? How will you remind yourself of this precision when you feel something in your life is chaotic or meaningless?

Sixth Proof: Sphere's Motion Refutes Point Tangency
Sanusi presents the sixth proof distinguishing rectilinear and rotational motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how distinguishing rectilinear and rotational motion in a sphere's movement refutes the point-tangency objection and defends atomism.

Core Idea
A sphere's rolling combines two motions, avoiding point contact and preserving atomism.

This passage presents the sixth of eight proofs defending the atomic structure of bodies. The objection claimed that a sphere moving on a surface would need point-to-point tangency, implying continuity. But by analyzing the sphere's motion as both rectilinear and rotational, the response shows that tangency is not at a single point, preserving the possibility of atoms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's claim: if tangency at the first point remains during rectilinear motion, the sphere would be stationary. Note that this assumes only one type of motion.
The objector conflates rectilinear and rotational components.
2
Grasp the dual-motion response
The response distinguishes rectilinear motion (translation) from rotational motion. While the rotational component keeps the same point of contact, the rectilinear component changes it gradually.
This is key to resolving the contradiction.
3
Learn the truth from al-Shifa
The passage states that during motion on a line, tangency is not at a point. This refutes the need for a continuous point of contact.
This is the core insight from Avicenna.
4
See the refutation of the Imam's argument
The argument that a straight line would result is refuted: the coincidence is gradual, not instantaneous, so only a curved line is necessary.
This preserves the possibility of a smooth sphere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (السادس: مستقيمة ومن وضعية والمماسة على النقطة الأولى باقية بالقياس إلى الحركة الوضعية وأما بالقياس إلى الحركة المستقيمة التي وقعت على السطح فكلا والسائل إنما أورد السؤال باعتبار هذه الحركة وقال : إنه لو كانت المماسة على النقطة الأولى باقية بالقياس إلى هذه الحركة كانت الكرة ساكنة بالقياس إلى هذه الحركة والمفروض تحركها بهذه الحركة، وأما ثانياً : فلأنه لو قرر السؤال هكذا إن المماسة بالنقطة المعينة على الأخرى لا تحصل إلا بعد الحركة ففي حال الحركة لا بد أن تكون الكرة ساكنة، وإن كانت النقطة الأولى من الكرة على النقطة الأولى من السطح كانت الكرة ساكنة. وإن كانت بنقطة أخرى على نقطة أخرى متوسطين بين النقطتين الأوليين والأخريين لزم خلاف المفروض لم يتجه أن يقال : المماسة الأولى باقية إلى حصول المماسة الثانية فإنها وقد تقررت بتبدل النقطة الأولى من الكرة. فالحق ما استفيد من الشفاء أن المماسة حال على الحركة على الخط وليس فيها مماسة على النقطة الكرة فرض الإمام من أنه لو ماست الكرة السطح بالخط لوجب أن ينطبق من الكرة خط على ما خطه السطح فيكون ذلك الخط مستقيماً لأن المنطبق على المستقيم مستقيم فتكون الكرة متصلة فمدفوع بأن استقامة الخط في الكرة إنما يلزم لو كان انطباقه على خط السطح دفعياً وأما إذا كان تدريجياً على ما هو اللازم هاهنا فإنما يلزم وجود الخط المستدير في الكرة والآخر فيه . ما قاله من ذلك
The sixth aspect: Rectilinear and rotational, and tangency at the first point remains with respect to rotational motion; but with respect to rectilinear motion that occurs on the surface, both are false. The questioner only posed the question considering this motion and said: If tangency at the first point remained with respect to this motion, the sphere would be stationary with respect to this motion, but the assumption is that it moves with this motion. And secondly: if the question is formulated thus: tangency at the specific point on the other does not occur except after motion; so during motion the sphere must be stationary. And if the first point of the sphere is on the first point of the surface, the sphere is stationary. And if it is with another point on another point mediating between the first two and the last two, it would contradict the assumption. It is not valid to say: the first tangency remains until the occurrence of the second tangency, for it has been established by the change of the first point of the sphere. So the truth is what is derived from al-Shifa: that during motion on the line, tangency is not at a point. The sphere is the assumption of the Imam that if the sphere touched the surface with a line, it would necessitate that a line from the sphere coincide with what the surface traces, so that line would be straight, because what coincides with a straight line is straight, so the sphere would be continuous. But it is refuted by the fact that the straightness of the line in the sphere would only be necessary if its coincidence with the line of the surface were instantaneous; but if it is gradual, as is necessary here, it only necessitates the existence of a curved line in the sphere and the other in it. What he said from that.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which distinguishes between rectilinear and rotational motion to defend atomism. The objector claims that if tangency remains at the first point relative to rectilinear motion, the sphere would be stationary. The answer is that the sphere has both motions: the rotational component keeps the same point of contact, while the translational component gradually changes it. The truth, taken from Avicenna's al-Shifa, is that during continuous motion on a line, there is no actual point of tangency; contact is not at a point. The passage then discusses an argument attributed to 'the Imam' (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi): if the sphere touched the surface with a line, that line would have to be straight (since it coincides with a straight line), implying the sphere is a polyhedron. But this is refuted because the coincidence is gradual, not instantaneous, so only a curved line is required. Thus the argument for atomism is preserved.

الحركة الوضعية
المماسة
الشفاء
الخط المستدير
الانطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rolling a sphere always involves a continuous point of contact with the surface.
Correction: A sphere's rolling motion combines translation and rotation; the point of contact changes gradually, and during motion there is no actual point of tangency.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what does the truth from al-Shifa state about tangency during motion?
A) Tangency always occurs at a point.
B) During motion on a line, tangency is not at a point.
C) The sphere must be stationary during rotation.
D) The line in the sphere becomes straight.

Key Takeaway
The sphere's dual motion shows that tangency is not at a point, refuting the objection to atomism.

?

How does this humble you and increase your certainty in His perfect design? Commit today to observe one natural phenomenon and reflect on its complexity as a sign of the Creator.
?
This proof shows that our initial assumptions about motion can be mistaken. In your own life, what is one belief you hold about Allah's creation or about your own self that might be oversimplified? How can you seek deeper knowledge from the Quran and sunnah to correct it? Resolve to study one topic in ‘aqeedah this week that challenges your current understanding.
?
The objection to atomism was refuted by distinguishing between two types of motion. In your spiritual journey, are you distinguishing between your outward actions and your inward state? Are both moving towards Allah, or is one static? Identify one outward deed and one inward intention you will consciously align today to be in harmony.

The Fifth Proof for Atomism: Lines and Points
Al-Jurjani presents the fifth proof for atomism based on line contact and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth kalam proof for atomism based on the contact of lines and how al-Jurjani responds to an objection about the continuity of motion.

Core Idea
The contact of a line on a line proves that bodies are composed of discrete points.

This lesson presents one of the most geometric arguments for atomism from the mutakallimun. By considering a line moving over another line, the proof demonstrates that the line must be composed of points, and by extension, surfaces and bodies are composed of discrete parts. An objection from the nature of motion and accidents is then addressed, deepening our understanding of kalam physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the structure: first the proof, then the objection, then the response.
The passage uses technical terms like مماسة (contact) and نقطة (point).
2
Understand the geometric argument
Why does contact at a point imply the line is composed of points? Consider that a line moving over another line can only touch it at a single point at any instant.
The proof relies on the idea that contact between two lines is always at a point, not along a length.
3
Identify the objection and response
The objection argues that a moving object must be divisible, but lines and points are accidents. The response invokes continuous motion and the potential nature of points.
This shows the sophistication of kalam physics in addressing objections.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This proof supports atomism: bodies are aggregates of atoms. It uses geometry to argue that continuity implies composition from indivisible parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الخامس : نفرض خطاً قائماً على خط ويمر) الخط الأول ( عليه ) أي على الخط الثاني ( فإنه يماس ( الخط المار ( في مروره جميع أجزاء ذلك ) الخط الممرور عليه أو المماسة ) بينهما ( إنما تكون بنقطة ) لأن المماس من الخط القائم المار هو طرفه النقطة الذي هو وممسوس النقطة لا يكون إلا نقطة ) فالخط الممرور عليه مركب من نقط) متتالية (و) كذلك (السطح) مركب من خطوط متلاقية (والجسم) مركب ) من سطوح ) مجتمعة ، ( وهو المطلوب ( ويتجه عليه أن المتحرك هو المتحيز بالذات فلا بد أن يكون منقسماً في جميع الجهات كما سيأتي فالسطح والخط والنقطة لا تكون إلا أعراضاً فكيف يتصور حركة خط عرضي على آخر مثله. قوله : ( ويتجه عليه إلخ ) وهكذا يتجه عليه أن الحركة متصلة منطبقة على المسافة المتصلة ليس فيها النقطة بالفعل إلا بعد فرض الآن في الزمان فتبين أن كل نقطتين مفروضتين خط كما أن بین کل آنین زمان وبين كل جزأين حصول في حد. قوله : ( فلا بد وأن يكون منقسماً في جميع الجهات كما سيأتي) أي في أول مقصد يليه.
The fifth aspect: we suppose a line perpendicular to a line and the first line passes over it—i.e., over the second line—for it contacts the passing line in its passage all the parts of that line passed over. Or the contact between them occurs only at a point, because the tangent from the perpendicular passing line is its endpoint which is a point, and the point of contact is not but a point. So the line passed over is composed of consecutive points, and likewise the surface is composed of contiguous lines, and the body is composed of surfaces aggregated. And that is what was sought. And it is objected that the moving object is essentially extended, so it must be divisible in all directions as will come, so surface, line, and point can only be accidents; how then can one imagine the motion of an accidental line over another like it? His statement: 'And it is objected, etc.'—thus it is objected that motion is continuous, coinciding with the continuous distance, in which there is no actual point except after positing an instant in time. So it becomes clear that between any two supposed points there is a line, just as between any two instants there is a time, and between any two parts there is a limit. His statement: 'And it must be divisible in all directions as will come'—i.e., at the beginning of the following section.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the fifth proof) from the mutakallimun for the existence of atoms and the composition of bodies from them. It proceeds by considering a line moving over another line. The author states: we suppose a line perpendicular to another line, and the first line passes over the second. During its passage, it contacts all parts of the line passed over. However, this contact is only at a point, because the tangent part of the moving line is its endpoint, which is a point, and contact with a point is nothing but a point. Therefore, the line passed over must be composed of consecutive points. The same reasoning applies to surfaces (composed of lines) and bodies (composed of surfaces). Thus the body is composed of discrete parts, which is the desired conclusion. An objection is then raised: a moving object that is essentially extended must be divisible in all directions, as will be discussed later. Surface, line, and point are only accidents, so how can one imagine a line (an accident) moving over another accident? The commentator responds by explaining that motion is continuous and coincides with a continuous distance; within this continuity, a point does not actually exist except when we posit an instant in time. Hence, between any two supposed points there is a line, just as between any two instants there is time. This shows that the objection is based on an assumption of actual points in motion, whereas motion is continuous. The gloss on the phrase about divisibility in all directions refers to a later section.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ (atom)
مماسة (contact)
نقطة (point)
خط (line)
سطح (surface)
جسم (body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof requires actual points in motion, i.e., that points are discrete entities existing in the line.
Correction: The response clarifies that points are only potential; in continuous motion, no actual point exists until we posit an instant in time.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth proof conclude about the line passed over?
A) It is continuous
B) It is composed of consecutive points
C) It is an accident
D) It is infinite

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that bodies are composed of discrete parts (atoms) through geometric reasoning about line contact, and the objection from motion continuity is resolved by noting that points are potential, not actual, in motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is composed of discrete, indivisible particles created by Allah, how does that change the way you think about the value and purpose of every moment you inhabit it? What specific habit can you adopt today to remind yourself that your existence is a continuous act of divine creation?
?
The objection to the proof claims that motion is continuous without actual points, but the atomist responds that points are only potential. In your own life, do you treat your spiritual growth as a continuous flow or as a series of discrete decisions? Where might you need to be more intentional about the 'points' of contact with your Creator?
?
The proof uses geometry to reach a conclusion about the unseen reality of bodies. When you reflect on the fact that even the lines you draw are composed of points you cannot see, how does this humble you in the face of Allah's infinite knowledge? What one assumption about the physical world do you need to surrender to trust in the unseen truths of revelation?

The Rolling Ball Proof: Two Motions and the Objection
Al-Jurjani states the sixth proof, presents two objections, and gives the answer from Avicenna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof for atomism involving a rolling ball, the objection raised against it, and the correct interpretation from Avicenna's Shifa' that contact occurs along the line of motion.

Core Idea
The ball's contact is along the line of motion, not at a single point.

This lesson examines the sixth proof in al-Jurjani's commentary, which uses a rolling ball to argue for atoms. The proof distinguishes between rectilinear and rotational motion. An objection claims that contact on a point would imply stasis, but the true answer shows that contact occurs along the line during motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the proof and objection
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Pay attention to the two motions and the initial claim about contact.
Notice the structure: proof statement, then two objections, then resolution.
2
Identify the two motions and contact condition
The proof states that contact on the first point remains for rotational motion but not for rectilinear motion. Why might this matter?
3
Understand the objection's two formulations
The objector presents two arguments: (1) if contact remained during rectilinear motion, the ball would be stationary; (2) contact on a point only occurs after motion, so during motion the ball must be stationary.
Both objections aim to show a paradox in the atomist view.
4
Learn the resolution from Avicenna
The truth from the Shifa' is that contact occurs along the line during motion, not at a discrete point. This resolves the paradox because contact is continuous along the line.
This is the key conceptual move.
5
Reflect on why this resolves the paradox
Consider how 'contact along a line' avoids the need for point-by-point contact, allowing motion without stasis at any instant.
Think about the difference between contact at a point vs. along a line.
