How Speed Variation Proves Atoms Are Divisible
Al-Iji argues speed variation entails divisibility of atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji uses the observable variation in speed and slowness to argue that what were thought to be indivisible atomic parts must actually be divisible.

Core Idea
Speed variation forces the divisibility of atoms.

Al-Iji presents a clever dilemma: either all motions are equal in speed, or atomic parts are divisible. Since we plainly observe motions varying in speed and slowness, the first option is false, so the second must be true. He then provides two rigorous proofs to show how this conclusion logically follows, both relying on the assumption that slowness is not due to pauses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a dilemma followed by two proofs.
The passage is dense and technical; read slowly.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the statement of the dilemma: either no variation in speed or division of indivisible parts. Understand why they cannot both be false.
The phrase 'preventing vacancy' means one must be true.
3
Trace the first proof using distance parts
Assume distance is made of indivisible parts. When a fast body covers one part, the slow body does not stop (since slowness is not due to pauses). It must move either a whole part (making it equal to the fast) or less than a part (forcing the part to be divided).
4
Trace the second proof using concurrent motions
Consider a fast motion and a slow motion that occur together and cannot be separated. When the fast covers two parts, the slow covers the same, implying divisibility.
This proof does not rely on the lack of pauses argument.
5
Reflect on the importance of 'no pauses'
Al-Iji insists slowness is not due to intercalation of rests. If it were, his argument would collapse. Consider why this assumption is necessary.
This is a key point often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : ما يتعلق بالسرعة والبطء وحاصله أحد الأمرين (لازم أي ثابت في الواقع على سبيل منع الخلو ( إما انتفاء تفاوت الحركات بالسرعة والبطء وإما تجزؤ الأجزاء ) التي لا تتجزأ فإنهما لا يجتمعان في الكذب لأن عدم التجزؤ يستلزم انتفاء التفاوت وعدم الانتفاء أعني وجود التفاوت يستلزم المتجزئ ) والأول ) وهو انتفاء تفاوت الحركات ( منتف ( ضرورة أن الحركات متفاوتة في السرعة والبطء ) فثبت الثاني)، وهو تجزؤ الأجزاء (بيان لزوم أحد الأمرين من طريقين: أحدهما أنه إذا تركبت المسافة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ فإذا ( قطع السريع جزءا منها ( فالبطيء لا يقف لما بينا من قبل ( أن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات فهو ) أي البطء إذن يتحرك فإما أن يتحرك جزءاً أيضاً فالسريع كالبطيء وهو الأول ) أعني انتفاء التفاوت فيما بين الحركات ( أو أقل من جزء) إذ لا مجال لتوهم حركته أكثر من جزء ( فيتجزأ ) الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لثبوت ما هو أقل منه ( وهو الثاني ) من الأمرين الذين أدعينا لزوم أحدهما وثانيهما) أي ثاني الطرفين المذكورين أن نبين أن ثمة حركة سريعة وبطيئة متلازمتين) بحيث يستحيل انفكاك إحداهما عن الأخرى ( فيستغني) حينئذ ( عن الاستعانة بأن البطء ليس لتخلل السكنات بل يكون ذلك ) أي تلازم هاتين الحركتين ( دليلاً على ذلك ) أي على أن البطء ليس للتخلل ( مستأنفاً ) كما نبهت عليه فيما مر وإذا كانت الحركتان متلازمتين ( فعندما تقطع السريعة جزأين قطعت البطيئة مثلها لزم تساوي السريعة
قوله : (على سبيل منع الخلو فإن التجزؤ والانتفاء متحققان معاً. قوله : ( بأنهما يقفان من وقف وقوفاً أي لا نسلم أنهما يلتقيان في الوسط بحيث يكون ذلك الوسط .
The third type: what pertains to speed and slowness. Its gist is one of two things (necessary, i.e., established in reality on the way of preventing vacancy): either the negation of variation of motions in speed and slowness, or the division of parts that are indivisible. For the two cannot be together in falsehood, because indivisibility entails negation of variation, and non-negation (i.e., existence of variation) entails divisibility. The first (i.e., negation of variation of motions) is negated, necessarily, because motions vary in speed and slowness; so the second is established, i.e., division of parts. Explanation of the necessity of one of the two things in two ways: One of them is that if the distance is composed of indivisible parts, then when the fast one covers a part thereof, the slow one does not stop, because we have already shown that slowness is not due to intercalation of rests. So it (the slow one) then moves. Either it also moves a part, so the fast is like the slow (that is the first, i.e., negation of variation among motions), or less than a part, since there is no room to suppose it moves more than a part. Then the indivisible part becomes divided by the establishment of what is less than it (and that is the second). And the second of the two mentioned alternatives is that we show that there exists a fast motion and a slow motion that are concurrent, such that it is impossible for one to be separated from the other. Then one dispenses with the assistance that slowness is not due to intercalation of rests, rather that (i.e., the concurrence of these two motions) becomes an independent proof for that (i.e., that slowness is not due to intercalation), as I have noted previously. And when the two motions are concurrent, when the fast one covers two parts, the slow one covers the like, so the fast one becomes equal to the slow.  He said: (On the way of preventing vacancy) For division and negation are both realized together. He said: (That they stop) from 'waqafa' meaning to stop; i.e., we do not accept that they meet in the middle such that that middle is [the juncture].
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage introduces a third type of argument regarding speed and slowness. The core claim is that either all motions are equal in speed (no variation), or atomic parts are divisible. Since motions are observably different, the second option follows. Two proofs are given. The first proof: if a distance is made of indivisible parts, when a fast body covers one part, the slow body does not stop (since slowness is not due to pauses). Therefore the slow body also moves. It must move either a whole part (making it equal to the fast) or less than a part (requiring division of the part). The second proof: there exist two motions, one fast and one slow, that are concurrent and inseparable. From this, one can show that when the fast covers two parts, the slow covers the same, leading to equality, thus proving that the parts are divisible. The marginal comments clarify that 'preventing vacancy' means both division and its negation are considered, and that atomists deny that the two moving parts meet exactly at a middle point.

تجزؤ
منع الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Slowness is due to intercalation of rests or pauses.
Correction: Al-Iji explicitly argues that slowness is not due to pauses; rather, it is due to moving less distance in the same time. This assumption is crucial for his proofs to work.

Checkpoint
What is the second possible outcome if motions vary in speed, according to the argument?
A) Motion is impossible.
B) The indivisible parts become divisible.
C) All motions become equal.
D) Slowness is due to intercalation of rests.

Key Takeaway
Observable variation in speed forces the conclusion that atoms, previously thought indivisible, must actually be divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Iji's argument shows that reality forces a logical conclusion that subverts a common assumption. When have you held onto a belief despite evidence against it? How can you train yourself to surrender to truth even when it challenges your comfort?
?
The proof that speed variation necessitates divisibility is built on the premise that two options cannot both be false. In your spiritual life, are there 'options' you avoid choosing between? What would it mean for you to accept the necessary conclusion that only God is indivisible and eternal?
?
Notice that slowness is not due to pauses in the argument. What 'pauses' or excuses do you use to avoid moving forward in faith? How can you eliminate those excuses and act with the speed of commitment?

Rotating Body vs. Atomism: Inseparable Motion
Physical argument against atomism using rotating body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the motion of a rotating body demonstrates that two points can move at different speeds without the body breaking, thus challenging atomism.

Core Idea
Different speeds do not break a body; inseparable motion preserves unity.

This lesson presents a physical argument from Islamic kalam against the atomist doctrine of indivisible particles. By examining a simple rotating body—a man turning on his heel—we see that two points can move at different rates while remaining connected. The argument challenges atomists to explain how a single body can have varied motion without tearing apart. We'll work through the passage step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: an example (rotating man), a rule (inseparability), an extension (celestial spheres), and objections with replies.
The passage includes glosses in parentheses; these are commentary inserted into the original text.
2
Understand the rotating man example
Visualize a man rotating on his heel, with his heel drawing a small circle and his fingertips drawing a large circle. Their movements are inseparable: when the fingertip moves, the heel also moves. If the heel stopped, the body would tear.
This is the core empirical observation.
3
Identify the logical implication
The inseparability of motions implies that the body remains continuous despite different speeds. Atomism, which posits indivisible parts, would predict tearing—but we observe no pain or breakage.
4
Consider the celestial analogy
The argument extends to two planets in the celestial sphere, one near the pole (slow) and one on the ecliptic (fast). Their motions are also inseparable; otherwise the spheres would rupture.
This strengthens the argument by appealing to known astronomical phenomena.
5
Review the objections and replies
The passage includes glosses that address potential criticisms: improbability is admitted but not refuting; pain only from natural separation; the necessity of circle formation is questioned; separation vs. deflection is distinguished.
These show the depth of the dialectical tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : الجسم البسيط واحد قابل للقسمة الصورة الثالثة من وضع عقبه على الأرض ويدور على عقبه فإنه يرسم دائرتين إحداهما بعقبه وهي أصغر والأخرى بأطرافه أكبر وإن شئت فافرضه أي الدائر على عقبه مادا باعه فرأس أصبعه يرسم دائرة أكبر بكثير من الدائرة التي يرسمها عقبه وحركتاهما متلازمتان لأنه إذا تحرك رأس أصبعه جزء لم يقف عقبه أصلا وإلا لزم تقطع ذلك الشخص على قياس ما مر ونحن نعلم بالضرورة أنه لا ينقطع جزءاً جزءاً كيف وتفرق الاتصال يوجب الألم مع أنه لا يجد ألماً أصلاً وإن شئت فافرضه أي رسم الدائرة الصغيرة والكبيرة في الفلك في كوكبين يدور أحدهما قريب القطب والآخر على المنطقة فإن حركتهما في رسم الدائرتين متلازمتان وإلا لزم الانخراق في الأفلاك وأن لا تكون موصوفة بالشدة والإحكام قوله : أبعد إلخ لا شبهة في الاستبعاد لكن الأمور الحقيقية تستلزم المستبعدات كامتناع الخلاء يستلزم أموراً يستبعدها العقل استبعاداً قريباً من الاستحالة قوله : كيف وتفرق إلخ تفرق الاتصال إنما يوجب الألم إذا كان طبيعياً ولا نسلم وجوده في ما نحن فيه قوله : فإن حركتهما إلخ فيه أن رسم الدائرتين إذا وقعت الأجزاء على وضع واحد بحيث يتصل على هيئة الدائرة ووقوعها على هذا الوضع حال التركيب ليس ضرورياً فلا تحصل الدائرتان ولو سلم فاللازم الانفكاك وهو غير الانحراف فإنه تباعد الأجزاء بعضها عن بعض والانفكاك لا يستلزمه ولو سلم فالانحراف جائز بل واقع عند أصحاب الجزء النقطة من حتى علم الأبطأ منها أنه كم ينبغي أن يقف حتى لا يزول عن سمتيه الذي كان له مع أن الإنسان على كمال فطنته يعجز عنه وقوله صغيره وكبيره يجوز بالنصف والرفع قوله أبعد من الانفكاك بين أجزاء الرحى فإن أجزاء الرحى لكونها في أحيازها لا يقتضي التناثر والتساقط لاقتضائها الأحياز التي يكون بعد الانفكاك
The first observation - the fifth objective: The simple body is one and divisible. The third image: He places his heel on the ground and rotates on his heel, so he draws two circles, one with his heel (which is smaller) and the other with his extremities (larger). And if you wish, suppose him (the one rotating on his heel) stretching out his arm, so the tip of his finger draws a much larger circle than the circle drawn by his heel. Their two movements are inseparable because when the tip of his finger moves a part, his heel does not stop at all; otherwise that person would be cut into pieces, by analogy to what has passed. And we know necessarily that he is not cut piece by piece. How? For separation of continuity causes pain, while he feels no pain at all. And if you wish, suppose it (drawing the small and large circle) in the celestial sphere with two planets, one moving near the pole and the other on the ecliptic. For their movements in drawing the two circles are inseparable; otherwise there would be a breach in the celestial orbs and they would not be described as strong and firm. His saying: (farther etc.) There is no doubt about the improbability, but real matters necessitate improbable things, like the impossibility of vacuum necessitating things that the mind deems improbable close to impossibility. His saying: (How and separation etc.) Separation of continuity only causes pain if it is natural, and we do not grant its existence in what we are discussing. His saying: (For their movement etc.) In it, that if the drawing of the two circles occurs with the parts in one position such that they connect in the form of a circle, their occurrence in this position at the time of composition is not necessary, so the two circles are not obtained. And even if it is granted, the necessary consequence is separation, which is not deflection, for it is the distancing of parts from each other, and separation does not necessitate it. And even if it is granted, deflection is permissible, indeed it occurs according to the proponents of the indivisible part. The point from which the slower one knew how much it should stop so that it does not depart from its alignment which it had, while man, despite his perfect intelligence, is incapable of it. And his saying: small and large may be in the nominative or accusative. His saying: (farther than separation between parts of a millstone) For the parts of a millstone, because they are in their places, do not necessitate scattering and falling because they necessitate the places that are after separation.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a physical argument against atomism (the doctrine of indivisible particles) by demonstrating that two points on a rotating body can move at different speeds while the body remains continuous and intact. The author describes a man rotating on his heel, drawing two concentric circles: a smaller one with his heel and a larger one with his fingertips. The movements of heel and fingertip are inseparable—any movement of the fingertip is accompanied by a corresponding movement of the heel. If the heel were to stop while the fingertip moves, the body would be torn apart, which is impossible because the person feels no pain and we know empirically that no tearing occurs. The argument is then extended to two planets in the celestial sphere: one near the pole moving slowly, another on the ecliptic moving quickly. Their motions are inseparable; otherwise the spheres would rupture, contradicting their firmness. The glosses that follow address potential objections. The first gloss admits the argument seems improbable but argues that real facts can imply improbable consequences (e.g., the impossibility of vacuum). The second gloss refutes the idea that separation of continuity necessarily causes pain—it only does if the separation is natural. The third gloss critiques the premise that parts necessarily compose a circle when rotating; if composition is not necessary, the argument fails, but even if granted, it entails separation (not deflection), which atomists might accept. The remaining glosses discuss technical points about the slowest motion and the grammar of 'small and large', and compare the separation of parts to a millstone, where parts in their proper places do not scatter.

جزء لا يتجزأ

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that different speeds in a single body require breaking or separation.
Correction: The rotating body argument shows that two points can move at different speeds while the body remains intact and continuous, refuting the idea that different speeds necessitate division.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The rotating body argument shows that two points can move at different speeds while the body remains intact, refuting the atomist claim that such motion necessitates division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you take time today to look for the underlying unity?
?
The argument uses a simple physical observation to challenge a deep-seated assumption about the nature of matter. ?

Sun and Shadow: Coordinated Motion at Different Speeds
Demonstrates coordinated motion at different speeds using sun and shadow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the motion of a shadow demonstrates that two bodies can move at different speeds without interruption, refuting the atomist claim of discrete motion.

Core Idea
Sun and shadow move together but at different speeds, proving continuous coordinated motion.

In this passage, the author presents a striking example: as the sun moves across the sky, the shadow of a stick shortens. The sun travels a huge distance while the shadow only moves a small amount, yet the shadow never stops moving. This continuous but unequal motion challenges atomist assumptions about motion being composed of discrete steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the mention of 'sound experience' – the argument relies on observation.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage claims that the sun moves much faster than the shadow, yet the shadow never stops moving. Find where this is stated.
3
Follow the proof by contradiction
The author argues that if the shadow stopped while the sun continued, the straight line connecting them would be impossible. Trace this logical step.
The proof uses geometry: a single straight line cannot connect two segments that are not aligned.
4
Consider the gloss on straightness
The gloss at the end questions whether 'real straightness' is needed. Reflect on how this qualifier affects the argument.
The gloss suggests the argument works even with a merely perceptual notion of straightness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة الرابعة : الشمس مع ظل الخشبة المغروزة حذاءها فإن الظل يقطع بالانتقاص من الصباح إلى الظهر قدراً من الأرض محدوداً كذراع أو ذراعين مثلاً والشمس في هذه المدة تقطع ربع فلكها فحركتها أسرع من حركة الظل بكثير من غير وقوف الظل عن الحركة لأن الشعاع الخارج من الشمس المار برأس الخشبة الواصل إلى طرف الظل إنما يقع بخط مستقيم كما تشهد به التجربة الصحيحة ووقوف الظل عن الحركة مع تحرك الشمس يبطل الاستقامة في الخط الشعاعي لأن الشمس إذا كانت في ارتفاع قد وصل منها خط شعاعي مار برأس الخشبة إلى طرف الظل على الاستقامة فإذا انتقلت إلى ارتفاع أعلى ولم ينتقص الظل أصلاً كان القدر الواقع من ذلك الخط فيما بين رأس الخشبة وطرف الظل باقياً على حاله وقد تغير ما كان منه بين الشمس والخشبة عن وضعه فلا يكون ذاك القدر الذي كان متصلاً به على الاستقامة في وضعه الأول متصلاً به كذلك في وضعه الثاني وإلا كان خط واحد مستقيم متصلا على الاستقامة بخطين ليسا في سمت واحد وهو باطل بالضرورة قوله : إنما يقع بخط إلخ وفيه أن الاستقامة الحقيقية محل بحث والاستقامة الحسية التخيلية ينافي عدم الاستقامة الحقيقية
The fourth image: The sun with the shadow of a stick planted in front of it. The shadow decreases from morning to noon by a limited amount of ground, like a cubit or two, and the sun in this period traverses a quarter of its orbit. So its movement is much faster than the movement of the shadow, without the shadow stopping its movement, because the ray emanating from the sun passing over the top of the stick and reaching the tip of the shadow falls only in a straight line, as sound experience testifies. And the stopping of the shadow from moving while the sun moves invalidates the straightness in the radial line because if the sun were at a certain altitude and a radial line from it passed over the top of the stick to the tip of the shadow in a straight line, then if it moved to a higher altitude and the shadow did not decrease at all, the portion of that line between the top of the stick and the tip of the shadow would remain as it was, while the portion between the sun and the stick would have changed its position. So that portion which was connected to it in straightness in its first position would not be connected to it in the same way in its second position; otherwise, there would be one straight line connected in straightness to two lines not in the same alignment, which is false necessarily. His saying: (It falls only in a line etc.) In it, that real straightness is a subject of investigation, and sensory imaginary straightness contradicts the absence of real straightness.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage provides a further argument against atomism by analyzing the motion of the sun and its shadow. As the sun moves, the shadow of a stick shortens: the sun moves much faster (a quarter of its orbit) while the shadow only moves a small distance. Crucially, the shadow never stops moving, showing that two motions (fast and slow) occur simultaneously in a continuous system. If the shadow were to stop, it would imply that the straight ray from the sun to the shadow's tip is broken, which is impossible. The author explains that if the sun rises higher without the shadow shrinking, the straight line would have to connect segments that are not aligned, which is absurd. The gloss clarifies that the argument relies on a notion of straightness that may be more mathematical than physical, but still serves to illustrate the point that coordinated motion can have different speeds.

خط مستقيم

Correction: The argument shows the sun moves much faster (a quarter of its orbit) while the shadow only moves a small distance, yet both move continuously without stopping.

Checkpoint
What would happen if the shadow stopped moving while the sun continued?
A) The stick would break
B) The radial line could not remain straight
C) The sun would fall
D) The shadow would shrink faster

Key Takeaway
Two objects can move at different speeds yet continuously coordinate because their connection follows a straight line that cannot be broken without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In your spiritual life, what feels like it is 'shadows' moving too slowly while your intentions race ahead? Are you willing to trust that the straight line of sincerity still connects them, even when you cannot see progress?
?
The proof shows that if the shadow stopped, the line would break. Is there an area of your obedience to Allah where you have allowed the 'shadow'—your outward actions—to stagnate while your inner state changes? What would it cost you to move that shadow, even a little, every day?
?
The sun's motion is continuous; the shadow's decrease is guaranteed. What is the one change you have been postponing because it seems too small or slow? Can you commit to moving that shadow starting today, knowing that the source of light (Allah's command) never stops?

The Bucket and Hook: Simultaneous Motion at Different Speeds
Demonstrates two objects moving at different speeds simultaneously to refute atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple mechanical example demonstrates that two objects can move at different speeds simultaneously, challenging atomist theories of motion.

Core Idea
Different speeds can coexist without pause, disproving atomist uniform steps.

This lesson presents a vivid example from early Islamic kalam: a bucket and a hook in a well move at different speeds yet reach the top together. The example is used to argue against atomist theories that motion must be composed of equal discrete steps. You will see the scenario, understand Al-Nazzam's misunderstanding, and grasp the logical implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic example and its translation to visualize the setup: a bucket at the bottom, a hook at the midpoint, and a rope connecting them.
Notice the glosses that clarify the physical arrangement.
2
Identify the key claim
Observe that the bucket and hook start moving at the same time, arrive together, but the bucket travels twice the distance of the hook.
This proves different speeds coexist without stopping.
3
Understand Al-Nazzam's error
Al-Nazzam mistakenly thought the speeds were equal and used the example to argue for the 'leap' (ṭafra) – a theory of discontinuous motion.
The example actually refutes his conclusion by showing different speeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة الخامسة : دلو على رأس حبل مشدود طرفه الآخر بوتد في وسط البئر مع كلاب يجعل في ذلك الحبل عند الوتد ويمد به فالدلو والكلاب يصلان إلى رأس البئر معاً فالدلو قطع مسافة البئر حين ما قطع الكلاب نصفه من غير وقوف للكلاب ضرورة فقد تلازمت حركة سريعة وبطيئة وقد توهم النظام تساوي هاتين الحركتين في السرعة فاستدل بذلك على الطفرة قوله : مع كلاب ظرف مستقر وقع حالاً من الضمير المستكن في الظرف أعني قوله على رأس حبل الراجع إلى الدلو وكيفية أن يكون الدلو مشدودة بطرف الحبل واقعاً في البئر ويكون الطرف مشدوداً بالوتد الذي في شط البئر ويكون الكلاب متعلقاً بذلك الحبل عند الوتد قائمة ذلك الحبل بالكلاب بأن يمد حبل الكلاب يكون وصول الكلاب والوتد معاً إلى رأس البئر وحركة الدلو سريعة لأنها قطعت كل مسافة البئر وحركة الكلاب بطيئة لقطعها نصف مسافة قوله : طرفه الآخر أي الطرف الآخر للحبل المذكور وقوله بوتر الوتر هاهنا خشبة أو حديدة معترضة في حلق وسط البئر وقوله مع كلاب هو بضم الكاف وتشديد اللام يقال له بالتركي جنكل قوله : فالدلو قطع مسافة البئر إلخ مثلاً إذا فرضناً بئراً عمقها مئة ذراع وفي منتصفها خشبة شد عليها طرف حبل طوله خمسون ذراعاً وعلى طرفه الآخر دلو ثم شددنا كلاباً معرب قلاب على طرف حبل طوله خمسون أيضاً وأرسلناه في البئر بحيث وقع الكلاب في الحبل الأول على طرفه المشدود في الخشبة ثم جررناه إلى رأس البئر فيكون ابتداء حركة الكلاب من الوسط والدلو من الأسفل معا وكذا انتهاؤهما إلى رأس البئر وقد قطع الدلو مئة ذراع والكلاب خمسين فقد تلازمت حركة سريعة وبطيئة كذا في المقاصد
The fifth image: A bucket on the end of a rope, the other end of which is tied to a stake in the middle of a well, with a hook (klab) placed on that rope at the stake, and it is pulled. So the bucket and the hook arrive at the top of the well together. Thus the bucket traversed the entire depth of the well while the hook traversed half of it, without the hook stopping necessarily. So a fast and a slow movement accompanied each other. And Al-Nazzam mistakenly thought these two movements were equal in speed and adduced that as evidence for the leap (ṭafra). His saying: (with a hook) is a circumstantial adverbial phrase referring to the implied pronoun in the adverb, i.e., his saying 'on the end of a rope' referring to the bucket. The manner is that the bucket is tied to the end of the rope, lying in the well, and the other end is tied to the stake at the edge of the well, and the hook is attached to that rope at the stake. Then that rope is pulled by the hook, so the hook and the stake arrive together at the top of the well. The bucket's movement is fast because it traverses the whole depth, while the hook's movement is slow because it traverses half. His saying: (the other end) means the other end of the mentioned rope. His saying: (by a stake) the stake here is a piece of wood or iron placed across the ring in the middle of the well. His saying: (with a hook) it is pronounced with damma on the kaf and tashdid on the lam; it is called in Turkish 'jenkel'. His saying: (the bucket traversed the depth of the well etc.) For example, if we suppose a well one hundred cubits deep, and in its middle a stake tied to one end of a rope fifty cubits long, and on its other end a bucket, then we attach a hook (klab), which is a قلاب, to the end of another rope also fifty cubits long, and we lower it into the well so that the hook catches the first rope at its end tied to the stake. Then we pull it to the top of the well. So the beginning of the hook's movement is from the middle and the bucket's from the bottom simultaneously, and likewise their arrival at the top. Thus the bucket traversed one hundred cubits and the hook fifty, so a fast and a slow movement accompanied each other. Thus in al-Maqasid.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a mechanical example to show that two objects can move at different speeds while starting and finishing together, without any stopping. A bucket at the bottom of a well is attached to a rope with a stake at the midpoint; a hook is attached to the rope at the stake. When pulled, both arrive at the top simultaneously, but the bucket travels the full depth (fast) while the hook travels only half (slow). The author mentions that Al-Nazzam (a Mu'tazili) mistakenly thought these speeds were equal and used this to argue for the 'leap' (ṭafra)—the idea that motion can skip over intermediate positions. The glosses clarify the setup: the stake is in the middle, the hook catches the rope, and both move together. The example demonstrates that two different speeds can coexist without any pause or discontinuity, refuting the atomist notion that movement must be composed of equal discrete steps.

حركة سريعة
حركة بطيئة
الطفرة
النظام

Misconception: Al-Nazzam thought the two movements (bucket and hook) were equal in speed.
Correction: The example shows they are different because the bucket travels twice the distance in the same time.

Checkpoint
What mistake did Al-Nazzam make regarding this example?

B) He thought both movements were equal in speed

Key Takeaway
The bucket and hook example proves that two objects can have different speeds while moving together, contradicting the atomist assumption of equal motion steps.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Nazzam clung to his theory of 'leap' even when a simple experiment contradicted it. Where in your life are you ignoring clear evidence because it doesn't fit your assumptions? Will you humble yourself today and let truth reshape your thinking?
?
The bucket and hook move together without one pausing for the other. Are you willing to move alongside others who are slower or faster than you, without resentment or impatience, trusting that Allah's design allows diverse paces toward the same goal?

Sixth Case: A Part on a Moving Line
Briefly presents and refutes the sixth case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the scenario of a part moving on a moving line is dismissed as a hypothetical exercise that does not refute atomism.

Core Idea
Not every imagined scenario is a valid objection.

This lesson presents the sixth and final case in the series of objections to atomism: a part moving along a moving line. The author quickly refutes it by noting that atomists do not accept that parts can exist in isolation, let alone move independently. Therefore, the objection is dismissed as a mere hypothetical exercise (تعليميات).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the terse style of the refutation.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the refutation's logic
The author argues that atomists do not assert the existence of parts in isolation. Therefore, any scenario involving an isolated part moving is irrelevant.
This is a key foundational belief of atomism.
3
Understand 'hypothetical exercise'
The term 'تعليميات' refers to suppositional reasoning used in teaching, not a real argument. Recognize that the objection is dismissed as purely imaginary.
This shows how the author categorizes invalid objections.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on why this case is the last one: it is the weakest objection, easily refuted by the atomist's own principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة السادسة : جزء يتحرك جزءا على خط متحرك جزء آخر في جهة حركة ذلك قوله : جزء يتحرك إلخ أصحاب الجزء لا يقولون بوجود الجزء على الانفراد فضلاً الحركة فهذا تخيل محض من قبيل التعليميات
The sixth image: A part moves on a moving line in the direction of that movement. His saying: (A part moves etc.) The proponents of the indivisible part do not assert the existence of the part in isolation, let alone its motion; so this is pure imagination of the kind of hypothetical exercises.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a final, brief argument and its immediate refutation. The sixth image suggests a part moving along another moving line. The gloss dismisses this as irrelevant because the atomists (proponents of the indivisible part) themselves do not believe parts can exist or move in isolation—they only exist within a body. Therefore, this imagined scenario is merely a hypothetical exercise (تعليميات) and does not constitute a real argument against atomism. The author uses this to show that the atomist position is not vulnerable to such abstract objections.

Key Terms

تخيل محض

Correction: The author clarifies that atomists do not believe parts can exist or move in isolation, so the scenario is based on an assumption they reject; it is merely a hypothetical exercise.

Key Takeaway

?

Proof of Divisibility from the Right Triangle
Jurjani presents the second proof against indivisible parts using the right triangle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple geometric proof using the right triangle and the Pythagorean theorem demonstrates the impossibility of indivisible parts by showing that the hypotenuse must be a fractional number of such parts.

Core Idea
Geometry reveals that even a right triangle forces division of the 'indivisible' part.

This passage presents a clever geometric proof against the concept of indivisible parts (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza). By constructing a right triangle with legs of ten parts each, the author uses the Pythagorean theorem to show that the hypotenuse cannot be a whole number of such parts. This forces the admission of fractional parts, thus disproving the idea that matter is composed of indivisible atoms. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Set up the triangle and theorem
Understand the triangle: a right triangle with legs 10 parts each. The Pythagorean theorem states that the square of the hypotenuse equals sum of squares of legs.
2
Calculate the squares
Compute each leg square = 100, sum = 200.
3
Find the hypotenuse range
Since 14^2=196 and 15^2=225, hypotenuse must be between 14 and 15.
4
Conclude fractional part required
Since hypotenuse is between 14 and 15, it must include a fraction of an indivisible part, proving parts must be divisible.
The proof shows that the 'indivisible' part cannot exist because the hypotenuse requires a fractional length.
5
Reflect on the implication
This shows that even a simple geometric figure contradicts the idea of indivisible parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني : مثلث قائم الزاوية كل من الضلعين المحيطين بالقائمة منه عشرة أجزاء فنقول : قام البرهان ( في شكل العروس ( على أن مربع وتره ) أي وتر قائمة المثلث ) كمجموع مربعي الضلعين ولكن مجموع مربع كل ضلع في المثلث المذكور مئة فمجموعهما مئتان فالوتر جذر مئتين بوانة فوق أربعة عشر جزءاً ( وأقل من خمسة عشر ) جزءاً وذلك لأن الحاصل من ضرب أربعة عشر في نفسها مئة وستة وتسعون والحاصل من ضرب خمسة عشر في نفسها مئتان وخمسة وعشرون فلا بد أن يكون جذر المئتين فيما بينهما ( فيلزم انقسام الجزء حينئذ أي الكسر الذي به يتمم الجذر المذكور . قوله : (فالوتر جذر مئتين جذر الشيء أصله وعشرة في حساب الضرب جذر مئة كذا في الصحاح يعني أنك إذا ضربت عدداً في نفسه فالمبلغ الحاصل من الضرب هو المجذور وذلك العدد هو جذر ذلك المبلغ، ثم إن الوتر الذي اعتبر كونه وتراً لقائمة المثلث المذكور لا بد أن يكون جذراً للمئتين وأنه يكون أكثر من أربعة عشر جزءاً وثمن جزء إلى ربع جزء.
The second aspect: a right triangle, each of the two sides surrounding the right angle is ten parts. We say: the proof has been established (in the form of the Bride) that the square of its hypotenuse (i.e., the hypotenuse of the right triangle) equals the sum of the squares of the two sides. But the square of each side in the mentioned triangle is one hundred, so their sum is two hundred. Therefore the hypotenuse is the square root of two hundred, [؟] above fourteen parts and less than fifteen parts. That is because the product of fourteen multiplied by itself is one hundred and ninety-six, and the product of fifteen multiplied by itself is two hundred and twenty-five. So necessarily the square root of two hundred is between them. Therefore the division of the part occurs then, i.e., the fraction by which the mentioned root is completed. He said: (so the hypotenuse is the root of two hundred): the root of a thing is its origin; and ten in the science of multiplication is the root of one hundred, as in al-Sihah. It means that when you multiply a number by itself, the resulting sum is the radicand, and that number is the root of that sum. Then the hypotenuse, which is considered the hypotenuse of the mentioned triangle, must be a root of two hundred and that it is more than fourteen parts and an eighth of a part up to a quarter of a part.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-wajh al-thani) against the existence of indivisible parts (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza). The author sets up a right triangle with legs of ten parts each. Using the Pythagorean theorem (called 'shakl al-arus' or 'Bride's figure'), he notes that the square of the hypotenuse equals the sum of the squares of the legs, which is two hundred. Since 14²=196 and 15²=225, the hypotenuse must be between 14 and 15, i.e., irrational. This means it cannot be an integer number of indivisible parts; it requires a fraction, implying that the part itself must be divisible to accommodate the irrational length. The commentary clarifies the terminology of square root (jadh) and confirms that the hypotenuse is slightly more than 14 parts, thus requiring a fractional part, which proves the divisibility of the assumed indivisible part.

Key Terms
الوتر
جذر
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
مربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an irrational hypotenuse is impossible with discrete parts.
Correction: The proof shows it necessitates fractional parts, thus indivisible parts cannot exist.

Checkpoint
In the right triangle proof, why does the irrational hypotenuse imply the divisibility of the part?
A) Because the hypotenuse is longer than the sum of the legs.
B) Because the hypotenuse cannot be expressed as an integer multiple of the indivisible part, requiring a fraction.
C) Because the legs are equal in length.
D) Because the square of the hypotenuse is a perfect square.

Key Takeaway
The Pythagorean theorem applied to a right triangle with integer legs yields an irrational hypotenuse, proving that indivisible parts cannot exist because a fraction of a part is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a seemingly self-evident assumption like 'indivisible parts' can be disproven by reasoning, what assumptions about your own faith or life might be similarly fragile? Are you willing to examine them with the same rigor?
?
The proof demands precise calculation and logical steps. When it comes to your beliefs, do you invest the same mental effort to verify them, or do you rely on what is convenient or traditional? What is one belief you can test today?
?
This argument forces a conclusion that may be uncomfortable: the world is not made of simple, indivisible building blocks. How do you react when your intellect leads you to a conclusion that contradicts your initial intuition? Will you humble yourself and accept the truth, or resist?

The Ladder Analogy: Setting Up the Proof
Jurjani describes the ladder analogy and the equal-motion assumption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric setup of the ladder proof against indivisible parts and how it models the motion of a right triangle.

Core Idea
The ladder proof uses a right triangle to show that indivisible parts lead to contradiction.

Jurjani presents the third proof, which uses a simple physical analogy: a ladder leaning against a wall. By analyzing the motion of the ladder as it descends, he sets up a geometric argument that will ultimately disprove the existence of indivisible parts. This lesson helps you visualize the setup before examining the logical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing the triangle and ladder
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation. Notice how the author describes the right triangle and the motion of the hypotenuse.
The passage uses the term 'وتر' for hypotenuse and 'رجل' for the base leg.
2
Visualize the right triangle
Imagine a right triangle with a vertical side (the wall) and a horizontal base (the ground). The hypotenuse is the ladder. Initially, the tip touches the top of the vertical side.
The wall is 'الضلع المنصوب'.
3
Understand the motion described
As the tip slides down the vertical side, the base slides away from the wall. The author emphasizes that whenever the tip descends by some amount, the base moves outward by some amount.
This sets up the key variables: descent (انحطاط) and drag (انجرار).
4
Identify the key assumptions
The proof considers what would happen if the descent and drag are equal. Notice that this is not yet stated as true—it's a hypothetical.
The equality assumption is the central point to be tested.
5
Prepare for the next lesson
The passage ends with the tip reaching the bottom. In the next lesson, we will examine the logical consequences of the equality assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثالث : هذا المثلث) القائم الزاوية ( إذا طبقنا رأس وتره أي وتر قائمته ( على ضلع) من ضلعي القائمة منصوب نحو السماء ( ومددنا رجله ) أي رجل الوتر ( من الطرف الآخر كسلم موضوع على جدار قائم على سطح الأرض يمد أسفله عن موضعه إلى خلاف جهة الجدار ) فلا شك أنه كلما ينحط من هذا الضلع ( المنصوب ( شيء ) والمقصود أنه كلما ينحط رأس الوتر عن شيء من هذا الضلع يخرج من ذلك الضلع شيء) أي يخرج رجله عن ذلك الضلع بشيء وهكذا إلى أن يصل رأسه إلى أسفل الضلع . فإن كان ( ما يخرج به أسفله ( مثله ) أي مثل ما ينحط عنه أعلاه ( لزم أن يكون الوتر مثل المنطبق على ضلع وهو الضلع الذي جر من طرفه أسفله لأن بعض الوتر منطبق على هذا الضلع (و) مثل ( الفاضل عليه ) أي على هذا الضلع أعني مقدار الانجرار ( وهو ) أي هذا الفاضل ( مثل ( الضلع (الآخر) إذ المفروض أن مقدار الانحطاط كمقدار الانجرار ( فيكون ( الوتر ( كمجموع الضلعين ويكذبه الحس والبرهان ) فوجب أن يكون مقدار ما ينجر إليه أقل مما ينحط عنه فإذا انحط جزءاً انجر أقل من جزء (وهذا ) الوجه ( يليق بالنوع الثالث من وجه ) وهو أن حركة الانحطاط أسرع من حركة الانجرار مع تلازمهما . قوله : (كلما ينحط من هذا المضلع شيء يخرج عن ذلك الضلع شيء) لفظ شيء في الموضعين قد وقع في أكثر النسخ مرفوعاً بدون الباء الجارة وفي بعض النسخ قد وقع مجروراً بالباء الجارة، وعلى النسخة الأولى أريد بالشيء في الموضعين ما هو طرف من الوتر وهو رأسه فقول الشارح: عن شيء من هذا الضلع أراد به بیان حاصل المعنى ولم يرد بالشيء هاهنا ما أراد به المصنف. وعلى النسخة الثانية أريد بالشيء الأول شيء من الضلع المنصوب فقوله: بشيء أي بمقدار من الضلع المنصوب وأريد بالشيء الثاني شيء من الضلع الفاضل على الضلع الأسفل فقوله : بشيء أو بمقدار من الضلع الذي فضل على الضلع الأسفل. وأما لفظ الشيء في قوله : عن ذلك الضلع بشيء فهو بالباء الجارة فيما وجدنا من النسخ وقوله : كمجموع الضلعين وهما الضلع المنصوب والضلع الأسفل بدون اعتبار الضلع الفاضل المساوي للضلع الأسفل فرضاً .
The third aspect: this right triangle, when we apply the tip of its hypotenuse i.e., the hypotenuse of its right angle onto a side from the two sides of the right angle erected towards the sky, and we extend its leg i.e., the leg of the hypotenuse from the other end, like a ladder placed on a wall standing on the ground, its base is moved from its place to the opposite direction of the wall. There is no doubt that whenever the tip descends from this erected side by something—and the meaning is that whenever the tip of the hypotenuse descends from something of this side, something emerges from that side, i.e., its leg emerges from that side by something, and so on until its tip reaches the bottom of the side. Then if what emerges from its base is equal to what descends from its top, then necessarily the hypotenuse would be equal to the side upon which it is applied—that is, the side from which its base was dragged—because part of the hypotenuse is applied onto this side, and equal to the excess over it, i.e., over this side, namely the amount of dragging, and this excess is equal to the other side, since it is assumed that the amount of descent equals the amount of dragging. Then the hypotenuse would be like the sum of the two sides, but sense and proof falsify this. Therefore, the amount of dragging must be less than the amount of descent. So if it descends by a part, it drags less than a part. And this aspect is suitable for the third type of aspect, which is that the movement of descent is faster than the movement of dragging while they are simultaneous. He said: (Whenever the tip descends from this polygon by something, something emerges from that side.) The word 'something' in both places occurs in most manuscripts as nominative without the preposition 'bi', and in some manuscripts it occurs as genitive with the preposition 'bi'. According to the first reading, by 'something' in both places is meant a part of the hypotenuse, i.e., its tip. So the commentator's saying 'from something of this side' intends to explain the meaning, and he did not intend by 'something' what the author meant. According to the second reading, by the first 'something' is meant something of the erected side, so his saying 'by something' means by an amount of the erected side, and by the second 'something' is meant something of the side that exceeds the lower side, so his saying 'by something' or an amount of the side that exceeds the lower side. As for the word 'something' in his saying 'from that side by something', it is with the preposition 'bi' in all copies we found. And his saying 'like the sum of the two sides' means the erected side and the lower side, without considering the excess side which is assumed equal to the lower side.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-wajh al-thalith) against indivisible parts, using a physical analogy: a right triangle is imagined as a ladder (sullam) leaning against a vertical wall (the upright leg of the triangle) on horizontal ground (the base). The hypotenuse is the ladder, its top touches the wall, its base touches the ground. When the top is slid down the wall by a certain amount, the base slides away from the wall by some amount. The author argues that if the base slides out by the same amount as the top descends, then the hypotenuse would equal the sum of the two sides (the wall segment from top to ground plus the base segment from wall to ladder foot), which is impossible both empirically and geometrically. Therefore, the base must move out by less than the top descends. Consequently, if the top descends by one indivisible part, the base moves by less than a part, implying that the part must be divisible to account for the smaller movement. The commentary discusses variant readings of the phrase 'by something' and clarifies the intended meanings, affirming that the proof relies on the assumption that descent and drag are simultaneous but of different speeds. This argument is categorized as the third type of proof (involving relative motion).

Key Terms
الوتر
الانحطاط
الانجرار

Key Takeaway
The ladder analogy models the motion of a hypotenuse descending a vertical side while its base moves horizontally, setting up an equality assumption to be tested.

The Logical Contradiction: Descent and Drag Cannot Be Equal
Jurjani deduces a contradiction from the equality assumption and concludes that drag is less than descent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why assuming equal descent and drag leads to a contradiction and how this disproves indivisible parts.

Core Idea
If descent equals drag, the hypotenuse would equal the sum of the two sides, which is impossible.

In the previous lesson, we set up the ladder analogy. Now we analyze the motion mathematically. Jurjani shows that if the base moves out by the same amount the top descends, then the hypotenuse would equal the sum of the two sides—a result contradicted by both sense and geometry. Therefore, drag must be less than descent, implying that indivisible parts are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage that derives the contradiction
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Focus on the conditional statement: 'if what emerges from its base is equal to what descends from its top...'
This is the heart of the proof.
2
Understand the logical derivation
Follow the reasoning: if descent equals drag, then the hypotenuse would equal the sum of the two sides. This is because part of the hypotenuse lies on the vertical side, and the excess over it equals the other side.
The passage explains this in detail.
3
Recognize the contradiction
The statement 'the hypotenuse equals the sum of the two sides' is false both empirically (sense) and geometrically (proof). Therefore, the assumption of equality is false.
This contradiction is the core of the proof.
4
Conclude that drag is less than descent
Since equal is false, drag must be less than descent. Consequently, if the top descends by one indivisible part, the base moves by less than a part.
This implies that the 'indivisible part' must be divisible to accommodate a smaller movement.
5
Relate to the broader proof
This is called the third type of proof, where descent and drag are simultaneous but of different speeds.
The passage notes that this aspect belongs to the third type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof relies on physical measurement or actual ladders rather than being a geometric argument.
Correction: The proof is essentially geometric and logical; the ladder is just an illustration. The contradiction is based on the known geometric truth that the hypotenuse is shorter than the sum of the other two sides.

The Circle Argument Against Indivisible Parts
Jurjani presents the fourth proof against indivisible parts using a geometric argument about the circumference of a circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the geometric properties of a circle are used to prove that indivisible parts (atoms) cannot exist.

Core Idea
A circle cannot be made from indivisible points without contradiction.

This lesson presents a clever geometric argument that was used by theologians to refute the existence of indivisible parts. By assuming a circle's circumference is composed of such parts, we derive a contradiction, showing that the atomistic hypothesis leads to absurdity. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Note that this is the fourth proof in a series.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the two cases
The proof hinges on a disjunction: either the outer part of each atom is larger than its inner, or it is not. Each case leads to division.
Case 1: outer > inner → atom has distinct regions → divisible. Case 2: outer not > inner → void or space appears → also divisible.
3
See the role of void
In the second case, void appears between atoms on the convex side. This forces the atom to have a part touching and a part not, hence division.
Void itself is not assumed to exist; it is a logical consequence of the arrangement.
4
Consider the commentary's nuance
Jurjani explains that atoms, if they are real substances occupying space, must have an outer and inner. This validates the disjunction and refutes the objection that atoms are dimensionless.
The proof assumes atoms are volume-occupying, not geometric points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع : بينا ) فيما تقدم ( وجود الدائرة ) وإمكانها مناف لوجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، كما يتبين من قوله : ( فإذا فرضنا دائرة فلو كان محيطها ) مركباً ( من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فإن كان ظاهر) تلك ( الأجزاء أكبر من باطنها ( حتى إذا تلاقت بظواهرها وبواطنها كان محدب المحيط المركب منها أكبر من مقعره ( انقسم الجزء ( لاشتماله على ظاهر أكبر وباطن أصغر ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن ظاهرها أكبر من باطنها ( فبين كل جزأين من أجزاء المحيط في جهة محدبه (إما خلاء) بأن تكون بواطن الأجزاء متلاقية دون ظواهرها فيلزم الانقسام في الجزء أيضاً لأن ما كان منه ملاقياً مغاير لما ليس بملاق على أنا نقول : ( فإن كان ) الخلاء الواقع بين كل جزأين بقدر ما يسع جزءاً كان (ظاهرها) أي ظاهر محيط الدائرة . قوله : ( فإن كان ظاهر تلك إلخ فيه أن هذا الترديد على وجود الظاهر والباطن المحيط للدائرة فإنه مركب من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ليس لها ظاهر وباطن، نعم أجزاء المحيط أكثر من أجزاء المحاط ولذا كان أوسع منه وهذا كما يقولون في محيط الدائرة على تقدير انتفاء الجزء فإنه ليس له ظاهر وباطن، بل خط غير منقسم محيط بالسطح أوسع من كل خط يفرض محاطاً به والفرق بأنه على تقدير الجزء جوهر متحيز بالذات، فلا بد له من ظاهر وباطن وهم ناشئ من قياس غير المنقسم .
The fourth aspect: we have previously demonstrated the existence of the circle, and its possibility contradicts the existence of the indivisible part, as becomes clear from his saying: So if we assume a circle, then if its circumference is composed of indivisible parts, then if the outer part of those parts is larger than their inner part, so that when they meet with their outer and inner sides, the convex part of the circumference composed of them is larger than its concave part, then the part divides because it contains a larger outer and a smaller inner. Otherwise, i.e., if its outer is not larger than its inner, then between every two parts of the circumference on its convex side there is either a void, such that the inner sides of the parts meet without their outer sides, then division also necessarily occurs in the part because what is in contact is different from what is not in contact. Moreover, we say: if the void occurring between every two parts is of a size that can accommodate a part, then its outer, i.e., the outer of the circumference of the circle... He said: (If the outer of those, etc.) In this, the disjunction is based on the existence of outer and inner for the circumference of the circle, for it is composed of indivisible parts which have no outer and inner. Indeed, the parts of the circumference are more than the parts of the interior, and therefore it is wider than it. This is as they say about the circumference of a circle on the assumption of the absence of the part: it has no outer and inner, but rather an indivisible line surrounding the surface, wider than any line assumed to be surrounded by it. The difference is that on the assumption of the part, it is a substance inherently occupying space, so necessarily it has an outer and inner, and this notion arises from analogy with the indivisible.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-wajh al-rabi') against indivisible parts, using the geometric properties of a circle. The author argues that the existence of a circle (which is possible) contradicts the existence of indivisible parts. The proof proceeds by assuming a circle whose circumference is composed of indivisible parts. Two cases are considered: (1) The outer part of each indivisible part is larger than its inner part. In this case, when the parts are assembled, the convex circumference is larger than the concave circumference, meaning each part has a larger outer and smaller inner, which implies the part itself is divisible (since it has distinct regions). (2) The outer part is not larger than the inner part. Then either the inner sides of adjacent parts meet while the outer sides leave a gap (void) between them, or the void is large enough to contain another part. In either subcase, the indivisible part would have a part that is in contact and a part that is not, again implying divisibility. The commentary by Jurjani refines this argument, noting that indivisible parts (as atomic substances) do have an outer and inner because they occupy space; otherwise, the circle would be a continuous line. Thus, the disjunction is valid. This proof shows that the very concept of a circle requires that its circumference be continuous, which is impossible if composed of indivisible points.

Key Terms
الدائرة
المحيط
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A circle can be approximated by discrete points; thus a perfect circle is not necessary for the argument.
Correction: The argument assumes a true geometric circle, not an approximation. If the circumference were composed of discrete points, it would not be a continuous curve, contradicting the definition of a circle.

Checkpoint
Why does the circle argument require that indivisible parts have an outer and inner?
A) Because atoms are three-dimensional.
B) Because they are substances occupying space (mutahayyiz bil-dhat).
C) Because the circle is a perfect shape.
D) Because the author says so without reason.

Key Takeaway
The circle argument shows that indivisible parts are incompatible with the existence of a true circle, thus refuting atomism.

The Fourth Proof: Detailed Version
Al-Jurjani presents the detailed version of the fourth proof against indivisible parts using a circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed reductio argument showing that if space consisted of indivisible parts, a circle could not exist, and why the existence of circles disproves atomism.

Core Idea
A circle's geometry logically contradicts indivisible space.

Al-Jurjani presents a fourth proof against the atomistic view that bodies are composed of indivisible parts. This proof leverages the geometric properties of a circle to show that if space were atomic, a circle would be impossible. Since we know circles exist, the premise of indivisible parts must be false. The argument proceeds through several branches, each leading to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the claim that the existence of a circle is incompatible with the indivisible part. This sets up the reductio.
2
Examine the first case: outer larger
If the outer parts of the circumference are larger than the inner parts, then the indivisible part would itself have a larger outer and smaller inner, implying division.
This is a direct contradiction to indivisibility.
3
Examine the second case: outer equal to inner
If outer and inner parts are equal, consider whether there is a void between them. Follow the subcases: void exactly fitting a part leads to the outer being twice the inner (denied by sense), void smaller leads to division, no void leads to infinite regress of concentric circles with equal parts.
All subcases produce contradictions.
4
Conclude the argument
Since every branch leads to a contradiction, the initial assumption that the circle's circumference is composed of indivisible parts must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع : بينا ) فيما تقدم ( وجود الدائرة ) وإمكانها مناف لوجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، كما يتبين من قوله : ( فإذا فرضنا دائرة فلو كان محيطها ) مركباً ( من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فإن كان ظاهر) تلك ( الأجزاء أكبر من باطنها ( حتى إذا تلاقت بظواهرها وبواطنها كان محدب المحيط المركب منها أكبر من مقعره ( انقسم الجزء ( لاشتماله على ظاهر أكبر وباطن أصغر ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن ظاهرها أكبر من باطنها ( فبين كل جزأين من أجزاء المحيط في جهة محدبه (إما خلاء) بأن تكون بواطن الأجزاء متلاقية دون ظواهرها فيلزم الانقسام في الجزء أيضاً لأن ما كان منه ملاقياً مغاير لما ليس بملاق على أنا نقول : ( فإن كان ) الخلاء الواقع بين كل جزأين بقدر ما يسع جزءاً كان (ظاهرها) أي ظاهر محيط الدائرة ( ضعف باطنها ( على ذلك التقدير ) والحس يكذبه ( فإن محدب المحيط، وإن كان أكبر من مقعره إلا أنه يستحيل أن يكون ضعفه ) وإن كان ذلك الخلاء ) أي كل واحد منه أو بعضه ( أقل ) من قدر يسع جزءا ( لزم الانقسام ) في الجزء لثبوت ما هو أقل منه . ( وإما لاخلاء، بأن تكون ظواهرها متلاقية كبواطنها مع أنه لا تفاوت بينهما، ( فيكون ) حينئذ ( باطنها ) أي باطن محيط الدائرة أو باطن الدائرة فإنها قد تطلق على محيطها ( كظاهرها) في المقدار (وهو) أي باطنها (كظاهر) دائرة (أخرى محاطة بها لانطباقها عليه ) وظاهر المحاطة أيضاً كباطنها ) لما عرفت في المحيطة ( وهي ) أي الدائرة المحاطة ) كثالثة ورابعة ) إلى دوائر أخرى ( بالغة ما بلغت فتكون أجزاء طوقية الرحى مثلاً كالقطبية ) منها ( وبطلانه لا يخفى ) . والأظهر في تقرير هذا أنه الوجه ما ذكر في الملخص من يمتنع جعل الخط المركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ دائرة، لأنا إذا جعلناه دائرة فإما أنه تتلاقى ظواهر أجزائه كما تلاقت بواطنها فيلزم أن تكون مساحة ظاهرها كمساحة باطنها، فإذا أحاطت بهذه الدائرة دائرة أخرى، كان حكمها مثل حكم الأولى، فيكون ظاهر المحيطة كباطنها وباطنها كظاهر المحاطة بها، لانطباقه عليه وظاهر المحاطة بها كباطنها، فيكون ظاهر المحيطة كباطن المحاطة بها، ثم هكذا تجعل الدوائر محيطاً بعضها ببعض بلا فرجة بينهما إلى أن تبلغ دائرة طوقها مثل طوق الفلك الأعظم، فلا تزيد أجزاء هذه الدائرة العظيمة جداً على أجزاء الدائرة المفروضة أولاً مع كونها صغيرة جداً، وإما أن لا تتلاقى ظواهرها مع تلاقي بواطنها فيلزم الانقسام لأن الجوانب المتلاقية غير الجوانب التي لم تتلاق، فظهر أن إمكان الدائرة ينافي وجود الجزء . قوله : ( والأظهر إلخ) لأنه أقل ترديداً ومقدماته أسهل بياناً.
The fourth proof: We have previously demonstrated the existence of the circle, and its possibility is incompatible with the existence of the indivisible part, as is shown by his statement: 'So if we assume a circle, and if its circumference were composed of indivisible parts, then if the outer part of those parts is larger than their inner part (so that when they meet at their outer and inner parts, the convex part of the composite circumference is larger than its concave part), then the part would divide because it includes a larger outer part and a smaller inner part. Otherwise, i.e., if its outer part is not larger than its inner part, then between every two parts of the circumference on its convex side there is either a void (by the inner parts meeting without their outer parts), which necessitates division in the part as well, because what meets is different from what does not meet. We say: if the void between every two parts is enough to contain a part, then its outer part (i.e., the outer part of the circumference) would be twice the inner part according to that estimation, but sense denies this—for the convex part, though larger than the concave, cannot be twice it. And if that void (each one or some of them) is less than enough to contain a part, division in the part is necessitated due to the existence of something smaller than it. Or there is no void, such that their outer parts meet like their inner parts with no difference between them, then their inner part (i.e., the inner part of the circumference or the inner part of the circle—for it may be applied to its circumference) would be equal in magnitude to its outer part, and its inner part is like the outer part of another circle encompassed by it because it coincides with it, and the outer part of the encompassed circle is also like its inner part, as you know regarding the encompassing circle. And the encompassed circle is like a third and fourth, up to other circles ad infinitim, so its parts become like the circumferential parts of a millstone, for example, like the polar parts, and its falsehood is not hidden. The clearest way to state this is what was mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas: It is impossible to make a line composed of indivisible parts into a circle, because if we make it a circle, then either the outer parts of its parts meet as their inner parts meet, necessitating that the area of its outer part equals the area of its inner part. So if another circle surrounds this circle, its ruling is like the first, so the outer part of the surrounding circle is like its inner part, and its inner part is like the outer part of the surrounded circle—because it coincides with it—and the outer part of the surrounded circle is like its inner part. Thus the outer part of the surrounding circle is like the inner part of the surrounded circle. Then circles are made encircling one another without gap until one reaches a circle whose circumference is like that of the greatest sphere, so the parts of this very large circle do not exceed the parts of the first assumed circle despite its smallness. Or their outer parts do not meet while their inner parts meet, necessitating division because the meeting sides are different from the non-meeting sides. So it is clear that the possibility of a circle is incompatible with the existence of the part. His statement: '(And the clearest, etc.)' because it has fewer alternatives and its premises are easier to explain.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-wajh al-rabi') from the existence of a circle against the view that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (juz' la yatajazza). The proof proceeds by reductio: if the circumference of a circle were composed of such parts, then either the outer parts are larger than the inner parts or they are equal. If larger, the indivisible part would itself have parts (a larger outside and smaller inside), contradicting its indivisibility. If equal, then between any two parts on the convex side there must be either a void or no void. If a void exists and is exactly large enough to contain a part, then the outer part would be twice the inner, which the senses deny; if the void is smaller, then the part would be divided by a smaller measurement. If no void exists, then the inner and outer parts coincide exactly, leading to an infinite regress of concentric circles with equal parts—so a huge circle would have no more parts than a tiny one, which is absurd. The commentator then cites al-Mulakhkhas for a clearer formulation: the impossibility arises because either the outer and inner parts coincide, leading to equal areas and an infinite regress, or they do not, leading to division. The key conclusion: the very possibility of a circle refutes the indivisible part. The note explains why this formulation is preferred: it involves fewer alternatives and easier premises.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
الدائرة
المحيط
الخلاء
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is purely geometric and has no ontological implications.
Correction: The proof directly refutes the atomistic theory of space, showing that if space were composed of indivisible parts, geometric shapes like circles could not exist. Since circles do exist, the theory is false.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate consequence if the outer parts of a circle's circumference are larger than the inner parts?
A) The indivisible part remains indivisible.
B) The indivisible part would itself divide.
C) The circle would become a square.
D) The void between parts becomes zero.

Key Takeaway
The existence of a circle refutes the possibility of indivisible parts in space, as shown by multiple contradictions.

The Fourth Proof: Streamlined Version from al-Mulakhkhas
Al-Jurjani presents a refined version of the fourth proof from al-Mulakhkhas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the simplified version of the fourth proof that uses an infinite regress of concentric circles to refute indivisible parts.

Core Idea
A simpler argument circles endlessly to the same absurd conclusion.

After presenting the detailed proof, al-Jurjani cites a more elegant formulation from al-Mulakhkhas. This version avoids the multiple subcases by directly showing that if a circle is made of indivisible parts, then either the outer and inner parts coincide, leading to equal areas and an infinite regress of circles with the same number of parts, or they do not coincide, leading to division. The conclusion is the same but with fewer alternatives and easier premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the streamlined proof
Read the opening statement: 'It is impossible to make a line composed of indivisible parts into a circle.' Understand the core claim.
2
Examine the first possibility: parts coincide
If the outer and inner parts of the circumference coincide, then the area of the outer equals the inner. This leads to an infinite sequence of concentric circles with equal areas and the same number of parts, so a huge circle has no more parts than a tiny one.
This is a clear absurdity.
3
Examine the second possibility: parts do not coincide
If they do not coincide, then the meeting sides are different from the non-meeting sides, causing division in the part.
Again a contradiction.
4
Appreciate why this version is preferred
The commentator notes that this version has fewer alternatives (two vs. many subcases) and its premises are easier to explain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الرابع : بينا ) فيما تقدم ( وجود الدائرة ) وإمكانها مناف لوجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، كما يتبين من قوله : ( فإذا فرضنا دائرة فلو كان محيطها ) مركباً ( من أجزاء لا تتجزأ، فإن كان ظاهر) تلك ( الأجزاء أكبر من باطنها ( حتى إذا تلاقت بظواهرها وبواطنها كان محدب المحيط المركب منها أكبر من مقعره ( انقسم الجزء ( لاشتماله على ظاهر أكبر وباطن أصغر ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن ظاهرها أكبر من باطنها ( فبين كل جزأين من أجزاء المحيط في جهة محدبه (إما خلاء) بأن تكون بواطن الأجزاء متلاقية دون ظواهرها فيلزم الانقسام في الجزء أيضاً لأن ما كان منه ملاقياً مغاير لما ليس بملاق على أنا نقول : ( فإن كان ) الخلاء الواقع بين كل جزأين بقدر ما يسع جزءاً كان (ظاهرها) أي ظاهر محيط الدائرة ( ضعف باطنها ( على ذلك التقدير ) والحس يكذبه ( فإن محدب المحيط، وإن كان أكبر من مقعره إلا أنه يستحيل أن يكون ضعفه ) وإن كان ذلك الخلاء ) أي كل واحد منه أو بعضه ( أقل ) من قدر يسع جزءا ( لزم الانقسام ) في الجزء لثبوت ما هو أقل منه . ( وإما لاخلاء، بأن تكون ظواهرها متلاقية كبواطنها مع أنه لا تفاوت بينهما، ( فيكون ) حينئذ ( باطنها ) أي باطن محيط الدائرة أو باطن الدائرة فإنها قد تطلق على محيطها ( كظاهرها) في المقدار (وهو) أي باطنها (كظاهر) دائرة (أخرى محاطة بها لانطباقها عليه ) وظاهر المحاطة أيضاً كباطنها ) لما عرفت في المحيطة ( وهي ) أي الدائرة المحاطة ) كثالثة ورابعة ) إلى دوائر أخرى ( بالغة ما بلغت فتكون أجزاء طوقية الرحى مثلاً كالقطبية ) منها ( وبطلانه لا يخفى ) . والأظهر في تقرير هذا أنه الوجه ما ذكر في الملخص من يمتنع جعل الخط المركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ دائرة، لأنا إذا جعلناه دائرة فإما أنه تتلاقى ظواهر أجزائه كما تلاقت بواطنها فيلزم أن تكون مساحة ظاهرها كمساحة باطنها، فإذا أحاطت بهذه الدائرة دائرة أخرى، كان حكمها مثل حكم الأولى، فيكون ظاهر المحيطة كباطنها وباطنها كظاهر المحاطة بها، لانطباقه عليه وظاهر المحاطة بها كباطنها، فيكون ظاهر المحيطة كباطن المحاطة بها، ثم هكذا تجعل الدوائر محيطاً بعضها ببعض بلا فرجة بينهما إلى أن تبلغ دائرة طوقها مثل طوق الفلك الأعظم، فلا تزيد أجزاء هذه الدائرة العظيمة جداً على أجزاء الدائرة المفروضة أولاً مع كونها صغيرة جداً، وإما أن لا تتلاقى ظواهرها مع تلاقي بواطنها فيلزم الانقسام لأن الجوانب المتلاقية غير الجوانب التي لم تتلاق، فظهر أن إمكان الدائرة ينافي وجود الجزء . قوله : ( والأظهر إلخ) لأنه أقل ترديداً ومقدماته أسهل بياناً.
The fourth proof: We have previously demonstrated the existence of the circle, and its possibility is incompatible with the existence of the indivisible part, as is shown by his statement: 'So if we assume a circle, and if its circumference were composed of indivisible parts, then if the outer part of those parts is larger than their inner part (so that when they meet at their outer and inner parts, the convex part of the composite circumference is larger than its concave part), then the part would divide because it includes a larger outer part and a smaller inner part. Otherwise, i.e., if its outer part is not larger than its inner part, then between every two parts of the circumference on its convex side there is either a void (by the inner parts meeting without their outer parts), which necessitates division in the part as well, because what meets is different from what does not meet. We say: if the void between every two parts is enough to contain a part, then its outer part (i.e., the outer part of the circumference) would be twice the inner part according to that estimation, but sense denies this—for the convex part, though larger than the concave, cannot be twice it. And if that void (each one or some of them) is less than enough to contain a part, division in the part is necessitated due to the existence of something smaller than it. Or there is no void, such that their outer parts meet like their inner parts with no difference between them, then their inner part (i.e., the inner part of the circumference or the inner part of the circle—for it may be applied to its circumference) would be equal in magnitude to its outer part, and its inner part is like the outer part of another circle encompassed by it because it coincides with it, and the outer part of the encompassed circle is also like its inner part, as you know regarding the encompassing circle. And the encompassed circle is like a third and fourth, up to other circles ad infinitim, so its parts become like the circumferential parts of a millstone, for example, like the polar parts, and its falsehood is not hidden. The clearest way to state this is what was mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas: It is impossible to make a line composed of indivisible parts into a circle, because if we make it a circle, then either the outer parts of its parts meet as their inner parts meet, necessitating that the area of its outer part equals the area of its inner part. So if another circle surrounds this circle, its ruling is like the first, so the outer part of the surrounding circle is like its inner part, and its inner part is like the outer part of the surrounded circle—because it coincides with it—and the outer part of the surrounded circle is like its inner part. Thus the outer part of the surrounding circle is like the inner part of the surrounded circle. Then circles are made encircling one another without gap until one reaches a circle whose circumference is like that of the greatest sphere, so the parts of this very large circle do not exceed the parts of the first assumed circle despite its smallness. Or their outer parts do not meet while their inner parts meet, necessitating division because the meeting sides are different from the non-meeting sides. So it is clear that the possibility of a circle is incompatible with the existence of the part. His statement: '(And the clearest, etc.)' because it has fewer alternatives and its premises are easier to explain.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-wajh al-rabi') from the existence of a circle against the view that bodies are composed of indivisible parts (juz' la yatajazza). The proof proceeds by reductio: if the circumference of a circle were composed of such parts, then either the outer parts are larger than the inner parts or they are equal. If larger, the indivisible part would itself have parts (a larger outside and smaller inside), contradicting its indivisibility. If equal, then between any two parts on the convex side there must be either a void or no void. If a void exists and is exactly large enough to contain a part, then the outer part would be twice the inner, which the senses deny; if the void is smaller, then the part would be divided by a smaller measurement. If no void exists, then the inner and outer parts coincide exactly, leading to an infinite regress of concentric circles with equal parts—so a huge circle would have no more parts than a tiny one, which is absurd. The commentator then cites al-Mulakhkhas for a clearer formulation: the impossibility arises because either the outer and inner parts coincide, leading to equal areas and an infinite regress, or they do not, leading to division. The key conclusion: the very possibility of a circle refutes the indivisible part. The note explains why this formulation is preferred: it involves fewer alternatives and easier premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress is not a genuine contradiction.
Correction: The infinite regress shows that an enormous circle would have the same number of indivisible parts as a tiny one, which contradicts our geometric intuition that larger circles have larger circumferences and thus more parts.

Checkpoint
In the streamlined proof, what follows if the outer and inner parts of the circle's circumference coincide?
A) The outer area is twice the inner area.
B) An infinite regress of circles with equal areas results.
C) A void must exist between parts.
D) The circle immediately divides.

Key Takeaway
The streamlined proof uses an infinite regress of concentric circles to show that indivisible parts cannot form a circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that a circle made of indivisible parts leads to absurdity. Are there beliefs you hold that, if followed consistently, lead to logical contradictions? Have you ever avoided following a line of reasoning because it might force you to change a comfortable but flawed belief? Will you commit to examining your foundations honestly, even if it feels unsettling?
?
If the existence of a circle is impossible without continuous parts, what does that imply about the Creator who designed a universe full of precise circles? Do you truly believe that the universe's design points to a single Creator, or do you still entertain doubts? This lesson challenges intellectual compromise—so, what areas of your faith are you treating as 'indivisible' and refusing to let God's truth penetrate?
?
Consider the infinite regress of circles—each one's outer area equals the next's inner area. In your spiritual life, are you caught in a regress of excuses, always thinking 'I'll change later'? This argument uses regress to prove impossibility. How many more cycles of procrastination will you allow before you act on what you know is true?
